Then a shuddering breath of relief.\r\n\r\nSalee lifted her hands. The woman slumped forward, weeping gratitude into her own lap. Behind her, the line of patients stretched down the Cauldron-Hold clinic hallway and out into the street. Thirty people. Maybe more. All of them hurting. All of them waiting for her.\r\n\r\nSalee stood, swaying, and gestured to the next patient. \"Come.\"\r\n\r\nA man with a shattered knee limped forward. Salee pressed her hands to the joint and felt his pain flood in—sharp bone-deep ache layered over old injuries that had never healed right. She took it. Pulled it into herself, let the warmth carry it away, left him whole and grateful and crying.\r\n\r\nNext patient. A child with fever, skin burning hot enough to blister. Salee took the fever into her own hands, let it bloom across her palms, carried it away into nothing.\r\n\r\nNext patient. A woman with a poisoned wound, blackened flesh eating up her arm. Salee took the rot, took the pain, took everything that hurt and made it hers instead.\r\n\r\nBy the time the sun set, her hands were glowing faintly even at rest. Copper-gold veins traced up her forearms, across her shoulders, down her spine. And when she looked in the mirror at the end of the tide, she barely recognized her own face.\r\n\r\nHer assistant—Kerel, a boy barely old enough to apprentice—brought her water. She drank without tasting it.\r\n\r\n\"You need to rest,\" Kerel said. His voice was careful, the way people spoke to someone they thought might break. \"You've been working since dawn.\"\r\n\r\n\"There are more patients,\" Salee said.\r\n\r\n\"There will always be more patients.\"\r\n\r\n\"Then I'll always work.\"\r\n\r\nKerel set the cup down and knelt beside her chair. \"Healer Salee. When did you last eat?\"\r\n\r\nSalee tried to remember. Couldn't. \"This morning?\"\r\n\r\n\"Three tides ago.\"\r\n\r\nShe looked at her hands. The copper-gold glow pulsed faint beneath the skin, like her veins had forgotten how to carry blood and were carrying light instead. When she pressed her fingers to her own chest, searching for pain—hunger, exhaustion, the bone-deep ache that came from working too hard—she felt nothing.\r\n\r\nNot because the pain wasn't there. Because she couldn't feel it anymore.\r\n\r\nShe'd taken so much pain from others that her own had become invisible. Drowned out. Lost in the flood of everyone else's suffering.\r\n\r\n\"Kerel,\" she said quietly. \"What does my pain feel like?\"\r\n\r\nHe blinked. \"What?\"\r\n\r\n\"My pain. What does it feel like?\" She looked at him, and her eyes were hollow. \"I can't remember.\"\r\n\r\nKerel didn't answer. Just stared at her, expression caught between pity and fear.\r\n\r\nSalee stood. Her legs held, barely. \"Send in the next patient.\"\r\n\r\n\"Healer—\"\r\n\r\n\"Send them in.\"\r\n\r\nThe door opened. An old man hobbled through, leaning on a cane, face twisted with pain. Salee gestured him forward and pressed her hands to his back.\r\n\r\nThe pain flooded in. Sharp. Chronic. Old. She took it all, pulled it into herself, added it to the vast ocean of suffering she'd been carrying for two anneals. It didn't hurt. Nothing hurt anymore. She was just a vessel now. A conduit. Pain went in, disappeared, and she remained hollow.\r\n\r\nBehind her, Kerel was saying something. She couldn't hear him. The copper-gold glow was too bright, the warmth too consuming, and somewhere deep inside, a voice whispered: *If I take all the pain, no one has to suffer.*\r\n\r\nThe old man left. The next patient came. Then the next. Then the next.\r\n\r\nBy the time the clinic closed for the night, Salee's hands were covered in copper-gold veins, her skin darkened along the lines like someone had traced them with molten metal. When she tried to speak, her voice came out flat. Empty.\r\n\r\nKerel helped her to the recovery room and laid her down on the cot. She stared at the ceiling and felt nothing.\r\n\r\nNo pain. No exhaustion. No hunger. No fear.\r\n\r\nJust the certainty that tomorrow there would be more patients. More pain. More need.\r\n\r\nAnd she would take it all.\r\n\r\nEvery last piece.\r\n\r\nUntil there was nothing left of her but the glow.\r\n\r\n***\r\n\r\n### **ERIES: ENDINGS ARE NATURAL**\r\n\r\nVenn Eries stood at the edge of the mourner's circle and spoke the words he'd written three tides ago.\r\n\r\n\"All things end,\" he said. His voice was soft, barely louder than the wind. \"The proof-wall ends. The riser ends. The city ends. And we—\" He gestured at the bodies laid out on the stone, wrapped in cloth, waiting for the pyre. \"—we end too.\"\r\n\r\nThe mourners wept. Some turned away. But most stayed, listening, because Eries's words had a weight to them. A gravity that pulled you in even when you didn't want to hear.\r\n\r\n\"Endings are not failure,\" Eries continued. He stepped into the circle, hands folded, gray robes trailing in the dust. \"They are honesty. The world lies to us. Tells us we can hold on forever. That if we just work hard enough, pray hard enough, love hard enough, we can stop the collapse. But we can't.\" He looked at the bodies. \"And pretending otherwise is cruelty.\"\r\n\r\nA woman in the circle—young, red-eyed, clutching a child's toy—stood. \"Then what do we do?\"\r\n\r\nEries met her eyes. \"We let go.\"\r\n\r\nThe words hung in the air, soft and gray and final. Some of the mourners nodded. Others looked away, unable to bear it.\r\n\r\nEries felt the pull. The tug at the edges of his chest, like something inside him was unraveling. He'd felt it ever since the Piercing—a whisper, a call, a certainty that everything was falling apart and there was no stopping it. Only accepting.\r\n\r\nHe raised his hand, palm out, and let the gray shimmer come.\r\n\r\nIt wasn't bright. Wasn't warm. It was soft, faint, the color of winter fog. It spread across the mourners like a blanket, easing the tension in their shoulders, the grief in their chests. Not erasing it. Just... releasing it. Letting it be what it was without fighting.\r\n\r\nThe woman with the toy exhaled. Her shoulders sagged. She didn't stop crying, but the crying changed—became softer, gentler, less like screaming and more like rain.\r\n\r\n\"Release is a form of love,\" Eries said quietly. \"To force a grip past its honesty is violence.\"\r\n\r\nThe gray shimmer faded. Eries lowered his hand.\r\n\r\nAnd felt the cost.\r\n\r\nHis fingertips were cold. Not just cold—numb, like the feeling had been drained out of them and would never come back. He flexed his hand and felt nothing. The numbness was spreading. Up his wrists now, creeping toward his elbows. By next tide, maybe his shoulders. By next anneal, maybe his chest.\r\n\r\nHe was teaching people to let go. And in doing so, he was letting go of himself.\r\n\r\nA voice behind him—Maerys, the temple elder who'd expelled him three tides ago for heresy. \"You're killing yourself.\"\r\n\r\nEries turned. Maerys stood at the edge of the circle, arms crossed, expression unreadable.\r\n\r\n\"I'm helping them grieve,\" Eries said.\r\n\r\n\"You're teaching them to give up.\"\r\n\r\n\"I'm teaching them to accept.\" Eries stepped closer. His voice didn't rise. Didn't need to. \"The world is broken, Maerys. It's not coming back. We can fight it—burn ourselves out trying to hold things together—or we can let it fall and make peace with what comes after.\"\r\n\r\n\"And what comes after?\"\r\n\r\nEries looked at his hands. At the gray patina spreading across his knuckles, the places where feeling had already fled. \"Honesty.\"\r\n\r\nMaerys shook his head. \"You used to believe we could rebuild.\"\r\n\r\n\"I still do. But not by denying what's broken.\" Eries met his eyes. \"Rebuilding starts with letting go of what can't be saved. Otherwise you're just building on top of rot.\"\r\n\r\nMaerys didn't answer. Just turned and walked away.\r\n\r\nEries watched him go, then turned back to the mourners. They were lighting the pyre now, flames climbing into the gray sky. The bodies burned. The mourners wept. And Eries stood at the edge, hands folded, feeling nothing but the certainty that all things end.\r\n\r\nIncluding him.\r\n\r\nBy the time the pyre burned out, the numbness had reached his elbows. He tried to feel his heartbeat and couldn't. Tried to remember what warmth felt like and came up blank.\r\n\r\nHe walked back to his quarters—a small room on the western outskirts, cold and spare—and sat at his desk. Opened his treatise. Read the words he'd written yesterday:\r\n\r\n*\"To hold past honesty is to strangle. Release is the kindest gift.\"*\r\n\r\nHe picked up his pen and added a new line:\r\n\r\n*\"This will age you. And it already has.\"*\r\n\r\nThen he set the pen down and stared at his hands. Gray. Numb. Empty.\r\n\r\nTomorrow there would be more mourners. More grief. More endings.\r\n\r\nAnd he would teach them all to let go.\r\n\r\nOne piece at a time.\r\n\r\nUntil there was nothing left of him but the lesson.\r\n\r\n***\r\n\r\n## **CHAPTER 12: DRIFT ALIGN**\r\n\r\nThe next morning, Tiffani woke before dawn and couldn't fall back asleep.\r\n\r\nShe lay on the cot in Bram's guest room, staring at the ceiling, listening to the distant ring of hammer on anvil. Bram was already working. He always was. But the forge was quiet otherwise—no Chloen, no refugees seeking repairs, no city officials demanding explanations. Just the steady rhythm of metalwork and the faint hiss of banked coals.\r\n\r\nTiffani sat up, flexing her hands. The stiffness was worse this morning, joints grinding audibly with each movement. The blue-silver patina had spread past her knuckles now, tracing up toward her wrists like frost climbing glass. When she tried to make a fist, her fingers only curled halfway.\r\n\r\nShe wrapped her wrists in the compression strips she kept beside the cot, then pulled on her boots and stepped into the forge.\r\n\r\nJhace was in the recovery room.\r\n\r\nShe found him sitting on the edge of the cot, hands unwrapped, staring at his palms. The copper-gold mark from the Piercing glowed faint even in the dim light, and the vein-line on his forearm looked dull—lifeless, like the glow had burned out and might never come back.\r\n\r\nHe looked up when she entered. His eyes were shadowed, exhausted, but when he saw her something in his expression eased.\r\n\r\n\"You're awake,\" he said.\r\n\r\n\"So are you.\" Tiffani crossed the room and sat beside him. \"How are you feeling?\"\r\n\r\n\"Like I tried to hold the world together and failed.\"\r\n\r\n\"You didn't fail. You're alive.\"\r\n\r\nJhace made a sound that might have been a laugh. \"Barely.\"\r\n\r\nTiffani looked at his hands. At the dull vein-line, the tremor that wouldn't stop, the way his fingers shook even when he pressed them flat against his thighs. Yesterday she'd watched him collapse. Watched the light fade from his hands, watched his eyes go empty, watched him forget her name. And for five breaths she'd thought she'd lost him.\r\n\r\n\"Let me see your arms,\" she said quietly.\r\n\r\nJhace hesitated, then held them out. Tiffani stood, crossed to the basin in the corner, and filled it with water from the cistern. She brought it back, set it on the bench beside the cot, and pulled a clean cloth from the supply shelf.\r\n\r\n\"What are you doing?\" Jhace asked.\r\n\r\n\"Washing you.\" She dipped the cloth in the water and wrung it out. \"You've been covered in soot and ash for two tides. And I need to see the damage.\"\r\n\r\nJhace didn't argue. Just sat still while Tiffani gently wiped the soot from his forearms, revealing the copper-gold vein-line beneath. It ran from his palm to halfway up his forearm now, dull but present. She cleaned it carefully, watching for signs of infection, for places where the skin had split or the burn had deepened.\r\n\r\n\"It's not getting worse,\" she said finally.\r\n\r\n\"It's not getting better either.\"\r\n\r\n\"No.\" Tiffani set the cloth aside and looked at him. \"But you're still here. And that's what matters.\"\r\n\r\nJhace was quiet for five breaths. Then he said, \"I couldn't remember your name.\"\r\n\r\n\"I know.\"\r\n\r\n\"I couldn't remember why I was there. What I was doing. I just—\" He stopped, jaw tight. \"It was like someone had scooped out everything that made me me and left only the hollow.\"\r\n\r\nTiffani felt her chest tighten. She'd seen that hollow. Seen the way his eyes had gone empty, the way his body had gone slack, the way he'd collapsed like a puppet with its strings cut. And she'd thought—for five terrifying breaths—that she'd lost him.\r\n\r\n\"You came back,\" she said quietly.\r\n\r\n\"Because you were there.\" Jhace looked at her, and his eyes were raw. Honest. \"I couldn't remember my name. But I could remember you.\"\r\n\r\nTiffani's breath caught.\r\n\r\nJhace reached up, slow and deliberate, and pushed back her sleeve. The burn-scar on her right forearm was visible now—a perfectly square mark, faded with time but unmistakable. The scar from the scaffold collapse. The scar from failing Marisse.\r\n\r\n\"What's this?\" he asked, though his tone said he already knew.\r\n\r\nTiffani looked at the scar. At the mark that had haunted her for three castings, the reminder of every miscalculation she'd ever made, every person she'd failed to save.\r\n\r\n\"I miscalculated a load,\" she said. Her voice came out flat. Empty. \"Three castings ago. I was working under Marisse—my mentor—on a scaffold repair in the Glass Quarter. I measured the beam wrong. Told her it would hold. It didn't.\"\r\n\r\nShe felt Jhace's fingers trace the edge of the scar, gentle and deliberate.\r\n\r\n\"She fell,\" Tiffani continued. \"Six rods. Broke her back. She lived, but she couldn't work anymore. Couldn't walk without pain. And it was my fault.\"\r\n\r\n\"It was an accident,\" Jhace said.\r\n\r\n\"It was a mistake.\" Tiffani pulled her arm back, wrapping her sleeve over the scar. \"And I swore I'd never make another one. That I'd measure everything three times, calculate every load, never trust anything I couldn't prove. Because if I was perfect, no one else would have to fall.\"\r\n\r\nJhace was quiet for ten breaths. Then he said, \"You can't be perfect.\"\r\n\r\n\"I know.\" Tiffani looked at him. \"But I tried anyway. And it made me—\" She gestured vaguely at herself. \"—this. Closed. Controlled. Alone.\"\r\n\r\n\"You're not alone anymore.\"\r\n\r\n\"No.\" Tiffani felt something break open in her chest. Something she'd been holding closed for three castings, locked tight behind walls of calculation and precision and fear. \"But I'm scared.\"\r\n\r\n\"Of what?\"\r\n\r\n\"Of losing you.\" The words came out raw. \"I watched you collapse yesterday. Watched you forget who you were. And for five breaths I thought—\" Her voice cracked. \"I thought you were gone. And I couldn't—I can't—\"\r\n\r\nJhace reached out and took her hand. His fingers were still trembling, still cold, but his grip was steady. Real.\r\n\r\n\"I'm here,\" he said.\r\n\r\n\"I know. But what if next time you're not?\" Tiffani squeezed his hand, hard enough that her knuckles ached. \"What if next time I'm not fast enough? What if I lose you the same way I lost Marisse?\"\r\n\r\n\"Then you lose me.\" Jhace's voice was quiet. Honest. \"But you don't stop living because you're scared of losing people. You just—\" He stopped, searching for words. \"—you hold on tighter while you can.\"\r\n\r\nTiffani stared at him. At his raw, exhausted face. At his trembling hands and dull vein-line and the copper-gold mark that would never fade. At the man who'd burned himself out trying to save everyone and had almost died because he couldn't let go.\r\n\r\nAnd she thought: *He's right.*\r\n\r\nShe leaned forward, slow and deliberate, and kissed him.\r\n\r\nIt wasn't careful. Wasn't calculated. It was just her, closing the gap, pressing her lips to his, tasting soot and ash and something warm underneath. Jhace froze for half a breath, then his hand came up to cup her jaw, his thumb brushing across her cheekbone.\r\n\r\nThe kiss was gentle. Tentative. Real.\r\n\r\nWhen they pulled apart, Tiffani's heart was hammering and her hands were shaking and she felt more terrified than she'd ever been in her life.\r\n\r\n\"I'm scared,\" she whispered.\r\n\r\n\"Me too,\" Jhace said.\r\n\r\nThey sat like that for ten breaths, foreheads touching, breathing each other's air. Then Tiffani pulled back and looked at him.\r\n\r\n\"Heat-Rib 3 still needs us,\" she said.\r\n\r\n\"I know.\"\r\n\r\n\"And the Guildhall's going to use the demonstration against us.\"\r\n\r\n\"I know.\"\r\n\r\n\"And we're probably going to burn ourselves out trying to stabilize it.\"\r\n\r\n\"Probably.\" Jhace smiled, tired and honest. \"But we're doing it anyway.\"\r\n\r\n\"Together,\" Tiffani said.\r\n\r\n\"Together.\"\r\n\r\n***\r\n\r\nThey spent the rest of the day practicing.\r\n\r\nNot with metal. Not with broken flanges or bent risers or shattered proof-walls. With each other.\r\n\r\nThey stood side by side in the forge's back room, hands clasped, and practiced moving in sync. Jhace shifted his weight left; Tiffani mirrored him. She turned right; he followed. They practiced breathing together, counting five breaths in and five breaths out, until their chests rose and fell in perfect rhythm.\r\n\r\n\"Your weight shifts before you move,\" Jhace said. \"I can feel it.\"\r\n\r\n\"Good.\" Tiffani adjusted her stance. \"Use it. If you can read me, you can follow me.\"\r\n\r\nThey practiced for two turns. By the end, they could move as one—Jhace's hands finding the fractures the moment before Tiffani pointed them out, Tiffani's shimmer following his warmth without a word spoken. It wasn't perfect. But it was theirs.\r\n\r\nWhen they finally stopped, the sun had set and the forge was dark except for the glow of banked coals. Tiffani's wrists were locked, joints grinding with every movement. Jhace's hands were shaking so hard he could barely hold them still.\r\n\r\nBut they were ready.\r\n\r\n\"Two tides,\" Tiffani said quietly. \"Then we do it.\"\r\n\r\n\"Proof or catastrophe,\" Jhace said.\r\n\r\n\"Proof or catastrophe.\"\r\n\r\nThey stood together in the darkness, hands clasped, and held on.\r\n\r\n***\r\n\r\n---\r\n\r\n## **CHAPTER 13: QUIET IGNITION**\r\n\r\nTwenty tides after the Piercing, Jhace Torrins stood in Bram's forge and watched the city wake.\r\n\r\nThe sun hadn't risen yet. The sky was the color of cold iron, bruised purple at the edges where the World-Vent had punched through and left scars that would never heal. But the Underface was already alive—torch-poles flickering, distant voices calling shift changes, the low hum of the heat-grid that was still holding, barely, after two anneals of desperate repairs.\r\n\r\nTomorrow they'd either save it or watch it die.\r\n\r\nJhace flexed his hands. The tremor was constant now, a baseline shake that never stopped. The copper-gold vein-line on his forearm glowed faint even at rest, tracing from his palm halfway to his elbow. His hands ached. His chest ached. Everything ached.\r\n\r\nBut he was still here.\r\n\r\nBehind him, footsteps. Tiffani appeared in the doorway, dressed in her work clothes—trousers patched at the knees, shirt rolled to the elbows, boots laced tight. Her wrists were wrapped in fresh compression strips, and when she moved her hands the joints ground audibly.\r\n\r\n\"You're up early,\" she said.\r\n\r\n\"Couldn't sleep.\"\r\n\r\n\"Me neither.\" She crossed the room and stood beside him, both of them staring out at the waking city. \"Tomorrow,\" she said quietly.\r\n\r\n\"Tomorrow.\"\r\n\r\nThey didn't say more. Didn't need to. Tomorrow was Heat-Rib 3. Tomorrow was the public demonstration. Tomorrow was proof or catastrophe, and there was no third option.\r\n\r\nTiffani turned back toward the main room. \"Come on. We have work to do.\"\r\n\r\n---\r\n\r\nThey spent the morning preparing.\r\n\r\nTools first. Jhace laid out his torque keys on the workbench—three sizes, each one worn smooth where his hands always gripped—and oiled the joints until they turned without catching. He checked his chalk, his plumb-drops, his hand-lamp. Everything needed to be perfect. Not because perfect mattered, but because having something to control helped quiet the part of his mind that was screaming.\r\n\r\nTiffani worked beside him, cataloging her own tools. Vernier. Theodolite. Spirit level. Plumb-line with the weighted bob she'd been using since her apprenticeship under Marisse. She cleaned each piece with a soft cloth, movements slow and deliberate, and packed them into her satchel one at a time.\r\n\r\n\"Your heat-bracer,\" Tiffani said suddenly.\r\n\r\nJhace looked up. \"What?\"\r\n\r\n\"The one your father made. You should wear it tomorrow.\"\r\n\r\nJhace hesitated. The heat-bracer hung on a peg near the forge—a wide copper cuff, hammered thin and etched with his family mark. His father had worn it every shift for two castings before the collapse. Jhace hadn't touched it since.\r\n\r\n\"It's just decoration,\" he said.\r\n\r\n\"It's protection.\" Tiffani crossed to the peg and lifted the bracer down. \"And it's a reminder.\" She held it out.\r\n\r\nJhace took it. The copper was cold in his hands, heavier than he remembered. He turned it over, tracing the etchings with one thumb—his family mark, the old forge symbol, the words his father had hammered into the metal: *Hold true.*\r\n\r\n\"Alright,\" Jhace said quietly. \"I'll wear it.\"\r\n\r\nTiffani nodded. Then she pulled her own satchel strap over her shoulder, adjusting the buckle until it sat just right. Jhace stepped forward without thinking and caught the loose end of the strap.\r\n\r\n\"Let me,\" he said.\r\n\r\nShe went still. Jhace threaded the strap through the buckle, cinching it tight so the weight distributed evenly across her shoulders. His hands brushed the back of her neck, and he felt her shiver.\r\n\r\n\"There,\" he said. His voice came out rough.\r\n\r\nTiffani turned. They were standing close—close enough that Jhace could see the blue-silver veining on her knuckles, the exhaustion shadowing her eyes, the way her breath hitched when she looked at him.\r\n\r\n\"Your plumb-drop's off-center,\" she said quietly.\r\n\r\nJhace glanced down. The weighted cord hung from his belt, twisted wrong. Tiffani reached out and adjusted it, fingers brushing his hip, straightening the line until it hung true.\r\n\r\n\"Better,\" she said.\r\n\r\nThey stood like that for five breaths, neither moving, both pretending they were adjusting tools and not just finding excuses to touch each other.\r\n\r\nThen Bram's hammer rang in the next room, breaking the spell, and they both stepped back.\r\n\r\n***\r\n\r\nBy mid-turn, the forge was packed.\r\n\r\nChloen arrived first, carrying a crate of salvaged flanges. Then refugees started trickling in—people who'd heard about the demonstration, people who wanted to help, people who just needed somewhere to be. Bram set them to work immediately, organizing supplies, sorting tools, carrying messages across the city to the Guildhall and the salvage yards.\r\n\r\nRumors swirled. Someone said they'd seen a healer in the Cauldron-Hold who glowed white-gold and took people's pain into herself. Someone else said a priest in the western quarter was preaching that all things end and you should let them. Jhace heard the names—Salee, Eries—and filed them away. Other people with the resonance. Other people burning themselves out trying to save a world that might not want saving.\r\n\r\nHe didn't have time to worry about them. He had his own burning-out to manage.\r\n\r\nBy evening, the prep was done. Tools packed. Plans finalized. Messages sent. Jhace and Tiffani stood in the empty forge, both exhausted, both too wired to rest.\r\n\r\n\"Come on,\" Tiffani said. \"I want to show you something.\"\r\n\r\nShe led him up. Not to the recovery room or the guest quarters, but higher—through a narrow maintenance shaft, up a ladder that groaned under their weight, onto the roof of the forge.\r\n\r\nThe city spread below them in all directions. The Underface stretched south, a lattice of iron and shadow lit by torch-poles that flickered like dying stars. The Glass Quarter rose north, kilns glowing cherry-bright even now. And beyond that, the Outer Crown climbed toward the bruised sky, proof-walls standing like broken teeth against the horizon.\r\n\r\n\"This is what we're holding,\" Tiffani said quietly.\r\n\r\nJhace looked. Really looked. At the people moving through the rubble like ants rebuilding a hill. At the heat rising from the risers they'd repaired, the marry points they'd stabilized, the structures they'd held together with nothing but will and copper-gold warmth and blue-silver geometry. At the city that was still broken, still bleeding, but still alive.\r\n\r\n\"Yeah,\" he said. \"It is.\"\r\n\r\nTiffani's hand found his. Her fingers were stiff, cold, barely able to curl around his palm. But she held on anyway.\r\n\r\nThey stood like that as the sun set and the torch-poles lit one by one across the Underface. Neither spoke. Didn't need to. Tomorrow was proof or catastrophe. Tomorrow they'd either save the city or watch it freeze. Tomorrow everything changed.\r\n\r\nBut tonight—tonight they just stood together, hands clasped, and held on.\r\n\r\n***\r\n\r\nWhen they finally climbed back down, the forge was quiet. Bram had banked the coals and gone to bed. The refugees had scattered to their shelters. Even Chloen had left, promising to meet them at dawn.\r\n\r\nJhace and Tiffani sat side by side on the bench outside the recovery room, too tired to move, too awake to sleep.\r\n\r\n\"You ready?\" Tiffani asked.\r\n\r\n\"No,\" Jhace said honestly. \"You?\"\r\n\r\n\"No.\"\r\n\r\nThey smiled at each other, tired and terrified and committed.\r\n\r\n\"Proof or catastrophe,\" Jhace said.\r\n\r\n\"Proof or catastrophe,\" Tiffani echoed.\r\n\r\nShe leaned her head on his shoulder. He wrapped an arm around her waist. And together, in the quiet darkness of Bram's forge, they sat and breathed and held on.\r\n\r\nTomorrow would come. The demonstration would happen. The city would judge them.\r\n\r\nBut tonight, they were just two people who'd found each other in the wreckage and decided to hold on anyway.\r\n\r\nAnd that—for now—was enough.\r\n\r\n***\r\n\r\n---\r\n\r\n## **CHAPTER 14: WEIGHT TEST**\r\n\r\nThe morning of the demonstration, Tiffani Merrow woke to hands that wouldn't work.\r\n\r\nShe lay on the cot in Bram's guest room, staring at her fingers. They were curled into claws, joints locked so tight she couldn't straighten them. The blue-silver patina had spread past her knuckles overnight, tracing up toward her wrists like frost climbing glass. When she tried to flex them, the joints ground audibly and didn't move.\r\n\r\nShe sat up slowly, every movement deliberate. Her chest was tight. Not from fear—though there was plenty of that—but from the simple, crushing knowledge that she couldn't do this alone.\r\n\r\nThe door opened. Jhace stood in the threshold, already dressed, his father's heat-bracer gleaming on his forearm. His hands were wrapped fresh, but she could see the tremor even through the cloth.\r\n\r\n\"You ready?\" he asked.\r\n\r\nTiffani looked at her hands. At the fingers that wouldn't straighten, the joints that wouldn't bend. At the tools of her trade rendered useless by two anneals of working herself to the bone.\r\n\r\n\"No,\" she said quietly.\r\n\r\nJhace crossed the room and sat beside her. He didn't ask what was wrong. Didn't need to. He just took her hands in his—gently, carefully—and started unwrapping the compression strips.\r\n\r\nThe blue-silver patina was unmistakable in the morning light. It covered her knuckles completely, spreading up toward her wrists, and when Jhace pressed his thumb to one of her joints it didn't give.\r\n\r\n\"How bad?\" he asked.\r\n\r\n\"I can't do precise work.\" Tiffani's voice cracked. \"I can't hold a vernier. Can't adjust a theodolite. Can't—\" She stopped, jaw tight. \"I can't do what I need to do.\"\r\n\r\nJhace was quiet for five breaths. Then he said, \"Then I'll do it for you.\"\r\n\r\nTiffani stared at him. \"You're Wholeness. You can't do Form.\"\r\n\r\n\"You taught me.\" He met her eyes, steady and certain. \"I trust your teaching.\"\r\n\r\n\"Jhace—\"\r\n\r\n\"We do this together.\" His voice was firm. \"You guide. I execute. We've done it a hundred times. This is just one more.\"\r\n\r\nTiffani felt tears well up and fought them back. \"What if you can't? What if I guide you wrong? What if—\"\r\n\r\n\"Then we die together.\" Jhace said it matter-of-factly, like it was the most obvious thing in the world. \"But we don't die alone.\"\r\n\r\nTiffani stared at him. At his raw, exhausted face. At his trembling hands and the copper-gold vein-line that traced halfway to his elbow. At the man who'd almost burned himself out trying to save everyone and was offering to burn himself out again—for her.\r\n\r\n\"Okay,\" she whispered.\r\n\r\nJhace nodded. Then he pulled a strip of soft cloth from his pocket and began wrapping her hands. Not tight, like the compression strips. Gentle. Supportive. Reminding her joints how to move without forcing them.\r\n\r\n\"You're going to be there,\" he said quietly as he worked. \"Right beside me. Telling me where to press, how much force, when to ease off. And I'm going to listen. The way I always listen.\"\r\n\r\n\"What if I mess up?\"\r\n\r\n\"You won't.\"\r\n\r\n\"How do you know?\"\r\n\r\nJhace finished wrapping her left hand and looked up. \"Because you're Tiffani Merrow. And you don't mess up.\"\r\n\r\nTiffani almost laughed. Would have, if her chest wasn't so tight. \"I messed up once. Marisse fell because of me.\"\r\n\r\n\"That was three castings ago. You're not the same person.\" Jhace started on her right hand. \"You're better. Stronger. And you're not alone anymore.\"\r\n\r\nThe tears came then. Tiffani didn't fight them. Just let them fall, silent and hot, while Jhace wrapped her hands and didn't look away.\r\n\r\nWhen he finished, he didn't let go. Just held her wrapped hands in his wrapped hands, both of them shaking, both of them broken, both of them still here.\r\n\r\n\"I'm scared,\" Tiffani said.\r\n\r\n\"Me too.\"\r\n\r\nShe leaned forward. Their foreheads touched. They breathed together—five breaths in, five breaths out—until their chests rose and fell in perfect sync.\r\n\r\n\"I love you,\" Tiffani whispered.\r\n\r\nJhace went still. For three breaths he just sat there, forehead pressed to hers, hands trembling in hers. Then he said, quiet and sure, \"I know. Me too.\"\r\n\r\nTiffani's breath hitched. \"You do?\"\r\n\r\n\"Since the forum. Maybe before.\" Jhace pulled back just enough to meet her eyes. \"You grabbed my hand and didn't let go. And I thought—if this is what dying feels like, I'll take it.\"\r\n\r\n\"We're not dying.\"\r\n\r\n\"No.\" Jhace smiled, tired and honest. \"We're holding on.\"\r\n\r\n***\r\n\r\nBy mid-turn, Heat-Rib 3 was surrounded.\r\n\r\nThe site sat at the northern edge of the Underface, where the main conduit bent toward the Glass Quarter. A crowd had gathered—refugees, salvage workers, Guild officials, even a few city council members wearing their brass chains of office. Someone had set up a platform for observers. Someone else had strung torch-poles in a wide circle, casting the whole site in flickering light.\r\n\r\nJhace and Tiffani stood at the edge of the circle, tools packed, hands wrapped, both silent.\r\n\r\n\"There's a lot of people,\" Jhace said.\r\n\r\n\"Yeah.\"\r\n\r\n\"If we fail—\"\r\n\r\n\"We don't fail.\" Tiffani's voice was firm. \"We hold it. Together.\"\r\n\r\nJhace nodded.\r\n\r\nBram appeared beside them, tool satchel over one shoulder. He looked between them, expression unreadable, then set the satchel down.\r\n\r\n\"Your father would be proud,\" he said to Jhace. Then he looked at Tiffani. \"And Marisse would say you finally learned to trust.\"\r\n\r\nTiffani felt her throat tighten. \"Thank you.\"\r\n\r\nBram nodded once, then stepped back into the crowd.\r\n\r\nChloen pushed through next, grinning despite her exhaustion. \"You two ready to save the world?\"\r\n\r\n\"Just the city,\" Jhace said.\r\n\r\n\"Close enough.\" Chloen squeezed his shoulder, then Tiffani's. \"Show them what partnership looks like.\"\r\n\r\nShe disappeared into the crowd. Jhace and Tiffani were alone again, standing at the edge of the circle, the weight of the city pressing down on them.\r\n\r\n\"Together,\" Jhace said quietly.\r\n\r\n\"Together,\" Tiffani echoed.\r\n\r\nThey stepped forward into the circle. The crowd hushed. The torch-poles flickered. And Heat-Rib 3 groaned, waiting.\r\n\r\n---\r\n\r\n---\r\n\r\n## **CHAPTER 15: LINE FUSE**\r\n\r\n\r\nHeat-Rib 3 was dying.\r\n\r\nThe crowd pressed in from all sides—refugees huddled three-deep behind the torch-pole circle, salvage workers leaning over the safety rail, Guild officials seated on the raised platform with their brass insignia catching the firelight. Derent sat front and center, expression carved from stone. Beside him, the gray-haired woman from the forum watched with cold, calculating eyes.\r\n\r\nJhace pressed his palm to the conduit and felt it immediately—the hollow hum, the fractured harmonics, the way the metal sang into empty space like it had given up trying to hold. The main artery was cracked in three places, stress fractures spiderwebbing across the surface. The pressure junction was sheared. The overflow valve was frozen shut. And underneath it all, a low groan that meant the whole structure was three breaths from catastrophic failure.\r\n\r\nTwo hundred risers depended on this conduit. If it failed, thousands would freeze.\r\n\r\nTiffani stood beside him, wrapped hands pressed to the metal, eyes closed. Her fingers barely moved—locked joints grinding audibly—but she was reading the structure, counting angles, calculating load paths in her head.\r\n\r\n\"Form first,\" she said quietly. \"The geometry's wrong. We straighten it, then seal the fractures.\"\r\n\r\n\"I can do that,\" Jhace said.\r\n\r\nShe opened her eyes and looked at him. Pale gray, sharp as cut glass. Terrified and certain. \"I'll guide. You execute. Just like we practiced.\"\r\n\r\nJhace nodded. He unwrapped his hands—the crowd gasped at the copper-gold vein-line that traced halfway to his elbow, glowing faint even in daylight—and placed them where Tiffani pointed. The main crack, three fingers deep, invisible from the outside but screaming in the grain.\r\n\r\n\"Feel for the angles,\" Tiffani said. \"Where the load wants to go. Not where it is—where it wants to be.\"\r\n\r\nJhace closed his eyes and reached for the blue-silver shimmer. Tiffani's way. It came reluctantly, cold and precise, nothing like the warm flood of Wholeness. But it came. He felt the geometry underneath—the angles, the stress points, the places where structure wanted to hold true.\r\n\r\nThe metal shifted. Slowly. The crack straightened, angles snapping back into alignment.\r\n\r\nSomeone in the crowd whispered, \"He's using Form. But he's Wholeness.\"\r\n\r\nAnother voice: \"They taught each other.\"\r\n\r\n\"Good,\" Tiffani whispered. \"Now hold it there while I—\"\r\n\r\nThe conduit groaned. Loud. The kind of sound metal made right before it gave up.\r\n\r\n\"Faster,\" someone in the crowd shouted. \"It's failing!\"\r\n\r\nJhace gritted his teeth and pushed harder. The blue-silver shimmer pulsed brighter, but his hands were shaking so hard he could barely hold the position. The tremor was getting worse, spreading up his forearms, into his shoulders—\r\n\r\n\"Jhace,\" Tiffani said, voice sharp. \"Don't force it. Guide it.\"\r\n\r\nHe exhaled. Eased off. Let the shimmer flow instead of flood. The metal stopped fighting. Settled. Held.\r\n\r\n\"Next fracture,\" Tiffani said.\r\n\r\nThey moved together. Jhace straightened the geometry at each crack while Tiffani called out angles and corrections. The crowd was silent now, watching, holding their breath. Guild officials leaned forward on the platform. Refugees pressed closer to the torch-poles, eyes wide.\r\n\r\nBy the third fracture, Jhace's hands were on fire. Not the warm glow of Wholeness—actual fire, searing pain climbing from his palms to his wrists. The copper-gold vein-line felt like it was burning through his skin, pulsing in time with his heartbeat.\r\n\r\n\"Almost there,\" Tiffani said. \"One more.\"\r\n\r\nJhace pressed his palms to the final crack and called the warmth. Amber light bloomed beneath his hands, seeping into the fracture, convincing the metal to remember wholeness. The crack sealed. Not perfectly—he could still feel the scar—but solid.\r\n\r\nThe conduit stopped groaning.\r\n\r\nThe crowd exhaled as one.\r\n\r\n\"Pressure junction,\" Tiffani said, already moving.\r\n\r\nThey worked faster now, hands moving in sync. Tiffani called out the corrections; Jhace executed them. Form to straighten, Wholeness to seal, a flash of white-gold Purity to scrape away the contamination eating through the valve housing. The crowd murmured. Someone gasped. A child asked, \"Are they glowing?\"\r\n\r\n\"Last one,\" Tiffani said. \"The overflow valve. It's frozen—stress has it locked. You'll need Entropy.\"\r\n\r\nJhace froze. Entropy. The principle he'd tried once and failed. The principle that had nearly killed him when he'd overreached.\r\n\r\n\"I can't,\" he said.\r\n\r\n\"Yes, you can.\" Tiffani's hand found his, wrapped fingers curling around wrapped fingers. \"You don't have to do it alone. I'm right here.\"\r\n\r\nJhace looked at her. At her exhausted face, her locked hands, the blue-silver patina covering her knuckles. At the woman who'd pulled him back from Hollowing, who'd taught him to let go, who'd said *I love you* and meant it.\r\n\r\n\"Together,\" he said.\r\n\r\n\"Together.\"\r\n\r\nThey pressed their hands to the valve—his left, her right, fingers intertwined. The crowd leaned in. Torch-poles flickered. Jhace reached for the gray shimmer. It came slowly, reluctantly, soft as winter fog. He felt the tension in the metal, the places where it was fighting itself, the stress that needed to release.\r\n\r\nHe whispered to it. Let it ease. Let the tension drain away like water flowing downhill.\r\n\r\nThe valve groaned—\r\n\r\n—and snapped.\r\n\r\nNot released. Snapped. The housing cracked clean through with a sound like thunder, and suddenly the whole overflow system was failing, pressure building, the conduit shuddering under the load—\r\n\r\nThe crowd screamed.\r\n\r\n\"No!\" Tiffani shouted.\r\n\r\nJhace didn't think. He threw everything he had into the metal—Wholeness flooding the crack, trying to seal it, trying to hold it together through sheer force—\r\n\r\nThe copper-gold glow blazed. His hands screamed. The vein-line on his forearm flared so bright it cast shadows across the torch-lit circle, and somewhere deep inside he felt the hollow opening again, the edge of Hollowing, the place where he stopped being Jhace and became just empty—\r\n\r\nTiffani's hand squeezed his. Hard.\r\n\r\n\"Not alone,\" she said through gritted teeth.\r\n\r\nHer blue-silver shimmer poured into the metal alongside his copper-gold warmth. Not separate. Together. Two principles layered, supporting each other, the geometry reinforcing the mend, the warmth filling the gaps the geometry left behind. Frost-light and amber heat spiraled together, braiding through the crack, holding where one alone would have failed.\r\n\r\nThe crack held. Barely. The valve shuddered, then steadied.\r\n\r\nBut it wasn't fixed. It was held. Barely. Temporarily. Good enough for today but not forever.\r\n\r\nThe conduit hum smoothed. Not perfect—there was still a faint third-note underneath, a memory of the break—but stable. Holding.\r\n\r\nHeat began to flow. Not a flood, but a steady pulse—warm, reliable, alive. The hum climbed through the conduit, up into the secondary trunk, spreading out through the marry points toward the Glass Quarter and the Outer Crown and the refugee shelters scattered between. The fractured harmonics resolved into something that almost sounded like music.\r\n\r\nThe crowd erupted.\r\n\r\nNot gasps this time. Cheers. Shouting. Someone yelling, \"They did it!\" A woman weeping. A child laughing. Refugees pounding fists against the safety rail, chanting their names: \"Torrins! Merrow! Torrins! Merrow!\"\r\n\r\nJhace lifted his hands. They were bleeding. The cloth wrappings had torn, and blood mixed with copper-gold light, dripping onto the stone. His vein-line glowed so bright it hurt to look at—three fingers long now, tracing all the way to his elbow. Permanent.\r\n\r\nBeside him, Tiffani swayed. He caught her before she fell, one arm around her waist. Her hands were locked completely now, fingers curled into permanent claws, the blue-silver patina covering her knuckles and spreading up toward her wrists like frost that would never melt.\r\n\r\nThe Guild officials rose from their seats. Derent stepped forward, expression unreadable. The gray-haired woman beside him looked between Jhace and Tiffani, then at the glowing conduit, then back.\r\n\r\n\"You stabilized it,\" Derent said.\r\n\r\n\"Barely,\" Jhace said. His voice came out rough. \"It'll hold for now. But it's not perfect. You'll need to reinforce it in the next anneal.\"\r\n\r\n\"You fixed it,\" Derent repeated. Louder this time, for the crowd. \"Heat-Rib 3 is stable. The city will have heat through winter.\"\r\n\r\nThe crowd erupted again. This time, sustained. Cheering, weeping, chanting. Someone started singing—an old Underface hymn, something about holding on. Others joined in.\r\n\r\nJhace didn't hear them. He was too busy holding Tiffani upright, feeling her weight sag against him, counting her breaths to make sure she was still conscious.\r\n\r\n\"We held it,\" he whispered.\r\n\r\n\"Barely,\" Tiffani said. Then she smiled, exhausted and honest. \"But we held it.\"\r\n\r\n\"And we still do.\"\r\n\r\nThe crowd pressed closer. Refugees reaching out to touch them, salvage workers pounding their backs, Guild officials arguing among themselves. Bram appeared at the edge of the circle, tool satchel over one shoulder, Chloen at his side. They pushed through the crowd, clearing a path.\r\n\r\n\"Come on,\" Bram said. \"Before you collapse in front of everyone.\"\r\n\r\nJhace let him take Tiffani's other arm. Together they walked through the cheering crowd, past the torch-poles, away from Heat-Rib 3 and its steady, imperfect hum. Behind them, the city moved. The heat-grid sang. Two hundred risers carried warmth through conduits that had been dying and now weren't—held together by two people who'd burned themselves out and were still here anyway.\r\n\r\n---\r\n\r\nThey didn't stay for the celebration.\r\n\r\nBy the time they reached the recovery room, Tiffani's eyes were closed and Jhace's legs wouldn't hold anymore. Bram laid them both down on adjacent cots, wrapped their hands in fresh cloth, and left them alone.\r\n\r\nJhace turned his head. Tiffani was already looking at him.\r\n\r\n\"We almost failed,\" she said quietly.\r\n\r\n\"But we didn't.\"\r\n\r\n\"Because you held on.\"\r\n\r\n\"Because *we* held on.\" Jhace reached across the gap between the cots. His wrapped hand found hers. \"Together.\"\r\n\r\nTiffani squeezed back. Her fingers barely moved, locked joints grinding, but she held on.\r\n\r\nOutside, the city moved. The heat-grid hummed. The torch-poles flickered. And somewhere in the wreckage, two hundred risers carried warmth through conduits that had been dying and now weren't—held together by two people who'd burned themselves out and were still here anyway.\r\n\r\nThe city had chosen its noise.\r\n\r\nAnd Jhace Torrins—hands bleeding, heart full, vein-line glowing faint beneath the bandages—understood: The bolt doesn't hold because it's tight.\r\n\r\nIt holds because it won't back out.\r\n\r\n***"

    "75e53ee0-861e-4b1d-85f0-5e0f1dadb7e0": {
      "children": [],
      "id": "75e53ee0-861e-4b1d-85f0-5e0f1dadb7e0",

          "metadata": {},
          "name": null,
          "role": "assistant"

        "channel": "final",
        "content": {
          "content_type": "text",
          "parts": [
            "# **THE GRIP THAT HOLDS**\r\n## N01: Turning One (Glass)\r\n\r\n\r\n---\r\n\r\n## **CHAPTER 1: SOMETHING WRONG**\r\n\r\nJhace Torrins pressed his palm flat to the heat-rib thirty-three cables below the Outer Crown, listening.\r\n\r\nMetal sang when it was happy. A good riser hummed blood-warm, a steady thrum you could feel through gloves if you knew where to lean. Junction 2–9 had sung clean for three castings—long enough that most of the Underface crew stopped checking the marry points where the old grid met the new. But Jhace checked. He always checked.\r\n\r\nHe closed his eyes and let the vibration climb through his wrist into his elbow, tracking the harmonic up the line. Bram stood two strides back with Chloen, both quiet. They'd learned not to interrupt when Jhace listened.\r\n\r\nThe hum was good. Steady. But underneath it, faint as a breath, something else: a third note, sour and low. He slid his hand three fingers to the left, then back. There. The off-note sharpened.\r\n\r\n\"Saddle-pin,\" Jhace said, straightening. He pulled his right glove off with his teeth and ran bare fingertips along the seam where the clamp-ring met the vertical. \"Bolt's backing out.\"\r\n\r\nChloen leaned in, eyes wide. \"I don't hear anything.\"\r\n\r\n\"You won't,\" Bram said, setting down his tool satchel with a leather thump. \"Not yet. Give it another anneal and the whole marry point rattles apart. Then you hear it.\" He looked at Jhace with the expression Jhace had learned to read as approval, though Bram never named it that way. \"Good ear.\"\r\n\r\nJhace pulled his torque key from his belt—a hand-long steel bar with an offset jaw, worn smooth where his thumb always rested—and fitted it over the bolt head. The metal was hand-warm, close to body heat but not quite. He gave the key a quarter-turn of pressure, testing. The bolt gave. Not much. A hair-width, maybe two. Enough to let the clamp-ring shift under load. Enough to let the hum go sour.\r\n\r\nHe set his weight and pulled. The bolt resisted, then caught, threads biting true again. He held it there for five breaths, letting the load settle, then chalked a white mark on the clamp face. Fixed. Checked. Held.\r\n\r\nChloen exhaled like she'd been holding it. \"That's it? Just tighten it?\"\r\n\r\n\"Just tighten it,\" Jhace said. He wiped chalk dust on his trousers and pulled his glove back on. \"Bolt doesn't care how smart you are. It just cares if you show up.\"\r\n\r\nBram chuckled, low in his chest. \"Your father used to say that.\"\r\n\r\nJhace didn't answer. He moved to the next marry point, three strides down the riser where it bent toward the secondary trunk, and pressed his palm flat again. His father had said a lot of things. Most of them were true. That didn't make them easier to carry.\r\n\r\nThe second joint sang clean. No sour note. He checked it anyway, running his hand along the seam, feeling for temperature differentials, for micro-shudders that meant a crack was starting somewhere invisible. Nothing. He marked it with chalk—a single vertical line, his shorthand for good—and moved on.\r\n\r\nThey worked their way down the riser in silence, Jhace leading, Chloen watching, Bram hauling the satchel and offering corrections only when the correction mattered. The Underface stretched around them in all directions: a lattice of iron and copper spanning two circuits end to end, holding up the city's weight. Proof-walls rose like stone teeth every five cables, twice as thick as a man was tall, anchoring the grid to bedrock. Torch-poles burned at every junction, casting long shadows that swayed when the wind pushed through the vent shafts.\r\n\r\nJhace loved it down here. Loved the smell of hot metal and old smoke, the way sound carried strange and layered, the fact that you could walk a whole shift without seeing another person if you timed it right. The grid didn't lie. Didn't leave. Didn't ask questions he couldn't answer. It just was, and if you listened, it told you what it needed.\r\n\r\nPeople were harder.\r\n\r\nBram cleared his throat. \"You planning to eat, or just work till you fall over?\"\r\n\r\nJhace glanced back. Bram had stopped beside one of the proof-wall alcoves, where someone had left a bench and a tin of flatbread. Chloen was already sitting, peeling the lid.\r\n\r\n\"I'll eat when we're done.\"\r\n\r\n\"We're done now,\" Bram said. He sat, knees popping audibly. \"Chloen, tell him.\"\r\n\r\nChloen looked between them, torn. \"We've checked eight junctions,\" she offered. \"That's a full morning.\"\r\n\r\n\"Nine junctions is a full morning,\" Jhace said. He pointed down the line, where the riser curved out of sight. \"Junction 2–10's a cable and a half that way. If the bolt's loose here, it might be loose there.\"\r\n\r\nBram broke off a piece of flatbread and chewed it slowly, watching Jhace with the expression that meant he was choosing his words. Finally he said, \"You're good with metal. Less good with people.\"\r\n\r\nJhace felt his jaw tighten. \"Metal doesn't leave.\"\r\n\r\n\"No. It just breaks when you don't take care of yourself.\" Bram gestured at the bench. \"Sit. Eat. We'll check Junction 2–10 after.\"\r\n\r\nJhace wanted to argue. The urge to keep moving, to check one more thing, to make sure nothing else was backing out somewhere unseen, sat in his chest like a clenched fist. But Bram was right. He'd been right about most things for the last three castings, ever since Jhace's parents had stopped being right about anything at all.\r\n\r\nHe sat.\r\n\r\nChloen handed him bread. It was still warm, proof someone had baked it within the tide. He ate without tasting it, eyes on the riser, tracking the line of torches north toward the Outer Crown. Somewhere up there people were eating in glass-houses with real windows, sitting in chairs that didn't smell like oil, talking about things that didn't matter. Down here, the grid held or it didn't. Simple.\r\n\r\n\"You ever think about going up there?\" Chloen asked, following his gaze.\r\n\r\n\"No.\"\r\n\r\n\"Liar,\" Bram said mildly. \"You think about it every time you look.\"\r\n\r\nJhace shrugged. \"Thinking's not the same as going.\"\r\n\r\n\"No,\" Bram agreed. \"But it's the same as wanting.\"\r\n\r\nJhace bit off another piece of bread and didn't answer. Wanting was dangerous. Wanting meant expecting. Expecting meant loss. He'd learned that the hard way, three castings back, when the proof-wall at Junction 5–14 had split and dropped two hundred stone of rubble onto the crew working underneath. His parents had been part of that crew. Jhace had been fifty cables away, checking a different site, listening to a different riser. He hadn't heard it happen. By the time he'd gotten there, the only thing left to hear was silence.\r\n\r\nHe'd stopped expecting after that. Stopped wanting. It was easier.\r\n\r\nChloen was saying something about the new Guild rosters, how they were expanding the repair teams, how maybe there'd be work up in the Crown districts soon. Bram was nodding along, asking questions. Jhace let their voices wash over him like background hum, neither close enough to matter nor far enough to ignore. He finished the bread and wiped his hands on his trousers, then stood.\r\n\r\n\"I'm checking Junction 2–10,\" he said.\r\n\r\nBram sighed. \"Jhace—\"\r\n\r\n\"I'll be back before the shift ends.\"\r\n\r\nHe walked before Bram could argue, boots ringing on the iron catwalk, tool belt settling into the familiar weight against his hip. Behind him, Chloen asked, \"Should we follow him?\" and Bram said, \"No. Let him work alone. He's good at it.\"\r\n\r\nJhace pretended not to hear.\r\n\r\nJunction 2–10 sat at the bottom of a secondary trunk, where three risers met in a star-pattern marry. The torch-pole here burned lower, half-spent, casting more shadow than light. Jhace lit his hand-lamp and clipped it to his belt, then crouched beside the central clamp.\r\n\r\nHe pressed his palm to the metal. Listened.\r\n\r\nThe hum was wrong.\r\n\r\nNot sour, like the bolt at 2–9. Worse. Hollow. Like the metal was singing into empty space, resonance bleeding off into nothing. He shifted his hand, pressing harder, trying to find the source. The riser was hand-warm, same as the others, but the vibration felt thin. Stretched. He pulled his torque key and checked the saddle-pin. Tight. He checked the clamp-ring. Tight. He checked the welds. Solid.\r\n\r\nBut the hum was still wrong.\r\n\r\nJhace sat back on his heels, frowning. He'd never heard a hum go hollow without a crack somewhere. But if there was a crack, it wasn't visible. He ran his hand along the seam again, slower this time, feeling for temperature shifts, for micro-vibrations that might betray a hidden split. Nothing.\r\n\r\nThe hair on the back of his neck stood up.\r\n\r\nHe froze, hand still pressed to the metal. That wasn't the grid. That was something else. A feeling, sharp and electric, like the moment before lightning struck. His mouth tasted copper. The air felt too thick, pressing down.\r\n\r\nHe looked up.\r\n\r\nFar above, beyond the lattice of iron and the proof-walls and the torch-lit junctions, beyond the Underface and the Outer Crown and the glass-houses glittering in the distance, something was falling.\r\n\r\nNo. Not falling. Descending. Impossibly large. Impossibly fast. A shape against the sky, darker than the clouds, cutting the air with a sound that wasn't sound—a pressure, a weight, a certainty that made Jhace's teeth ache.\r\n\r\nThe hum beneath his palm changed. Deepened. Became a roar.\r\n\r\nJhace stood, hand still on the riser, unable to look away. The shape grew. The roar grew. The copper taste sharpened into iron, into blood, into something that scraped the inside of his skull and demanded he run, now, move, but he couldn't. His boots were rooted. His breath was gone.\r\n\r\nThe thing in the sky was a spear. A column. A lance of impossible size, fused glass and iron and something molten that glowed orange-white at the edges, descending on Te'Oga like a hammer on an anvil.\r\n\r\nJhace thought, absurdly, I should have checked Junction 2–11.\r\n\r\nThen the world shattered.\r\n\r\n---\r\n\r\n## **CHAPTER 2: WARPED HORIZON**\r\n\r\nTiffani Merrow set the vernier against the stone sill and squinted through the sight-hole, waiting for the horizon line to settle.\r\n\r\nIt didn't.\r\n\r\nShe exhaled slowly, counted five breaths, and looked again. The line still sat a finger-width too high on the left, too low on the right. A warp. Subtle, but there. She'd measured it four times now, each from a different corner of the glass-house observatory, and the numbers came back identical. Not close. Identical. Which meant either her tools were perfect and the world had bent, or she'd lost her mind.\r\n\r\nShe straightened, pressing one hand to the small of her back where an old ache lived, and looked out across the city. The Glass Quarter spread below her in tiers: workshops with their tall chimneys, kilns glowing cherry-bright even in daylight, apprentice quarters stacked like children's blocks along the outer wall. Beyond that, the Outer Crown rose in a slow curve, proof-walls climbing toward the sky, their iron bracings catching the light. Fifty cables away—she'd measured the distance three castings ago, when the observatory was first built—the Underface began, a dark lattice of shadow and smoke where the city's spine lived.\r\n\r\nEverything looked normal. Everything looked wrong.\r\n\r\nTiffani turned back to her instruments. The plumb-line hung true, weighted with a finger-sized lead bob that didn't so much as tremble. The spirit level showed flat. The theodolite—brass and glass, heavy enough to dent a man's skull—sat centered on its tripod, calibrated that morning to three decimal marks. She ran her hand along the vernier's brass rail, checking for dust, for bends, for any excuse that would explain the readings away.\r\n\r\nNothing.\r\n\r\nShe pulled her logbook from the satchel at her hip and flipped to the morning's entries. Column one: angle of declination, measured from the eastern sill. Column two: angular offset, measured from the western. Column three: horizon shift, calculated. The numbers sat in neat rows, her handwriting small and precise. Four measurements. Four identical results. The horizon had warped by three arc-spans, west to east, in the space between dawn and mid-turn.\r\n\r\nHorizons didn't warp.\r\n\r\nNot unless the ground moved. Not unless something so heavy, so vast, so utterly impossible had pressed down on Te'Oga's crust that the stone itself bent under the weight. And even then, the warp would be visible. You'd see buildings tilt. You'd feel the ground shift. You wouldn't just get numbers that made no sense.\r\n\r\nTiffani closed the logbook and pressed it flat against her chest, eyes on the horizon. She'd been working alone for three castings now, ever since the last junior apprentice had left. He'd asked her once, in the careful tone people used when they thought you might be fragile, why she never took collaborators. She'd answered honestly: \"Precision requires focus. People are… imprecise.\"\r\n\r\nHe'd left two tides later.\r\n\r\nShe didn't miss him. People left. They always did. Numbers didn't. Numbers stayed true, stayed honest, stayed exactly where you put them. You could trust numbers.\r\n\r\nExcept when they told you the world had bent.\r\n\r\nTiffani set the logbook down and moved to the southern sill, where the second theodolite sat under its canvas cover. She pulled the cover free, folded it with sharp, deliberate motions, and tucked it into the storage crate. Then she bent to the eyepiece and began the alignment sequence again. Level the base. Center the crosshairs. Mark the horizon. Read the angle.\r\n\r\nThree arc-spans, west to east.\r\n\r\nShe straightened, jaw tight. Her hands didn't shake—she never let them shake—but something cold was pooling in her chest, spreading outward like frost on glass. The readings were impossible. But they were also correct. Which meant the world had changed, was changing, and she was the only one who'd noticed.\r\n\r\nOr the only one willing to measure it.\r\n\r\nBehind her, the observatory door creaked open. Tiffani turned, already composing the dismissal, and found Aedric standing in the threshold. He was younger than her by a full casting, thin and nervous in the way of apprentices who'd only recently been trusted with real tools. He held a sealed tube in both hands like it might explode.\r\n\r\n\"Senior Merrow,\" he said, and his voice cracked halfway through her name. He cleared his throat. \"The Guildhall sent this. They said it's urgent.\"\r\n\r\nTiffani crossed the room and took the tube without a word. It was glass, capped with wax, the kind used for priority messages. She broke the seal and pulled the parchment free, unrolling it with her thumbs.\r\n\r\n*All Guild members: Report structural anomalies immediately. Measurement priority. Do not delay.*\r\n\r\nShe read it twice, then looked up at Aedric. \"When did this arrive?\"\r\n\r\n\"A turn ago. Maybe less. They're sending runners to every glass-house in the Quarter.\"\r\n\r\n\"Did they say why?\"\r\n\r\nHe shook his head. \"Just that something's wrong with the proofs. That the marry points aren't holding right.\"\r\n\r\nTiffani folded the parchment and slid it back into the tube. Marry points. The junctions where old grid met new, where heat-risers connected to the proof-walls and the whole grid sang or didn't. She'd studied marry-point geometry for two full anneals under Marisse, learning to calculate load distribution to the third decimal. Marry points didn't fail unless someone had miscalculated.\r\n\r\nOr unless the ground moved.\r\n\r\nShe turned back to the theodolite, pulse climbing. \"Aedric. Come here.\"\r\n\r\nHe crossed the room in four strides, boots loud on the stone floor. Tiffani gestured at the eyepiece. \"Look through. Tell me what you see.\"\r\n\r\nHe bent, squinting, and was silent for five breaths. Then he straightened, frowning. \"The horizon's off.\"\r\n\r\n\"By how much?\"\r\n\r\n\"I don't—\" He stopped, glanced at her, then bent again. \"Three arc-spans, maybe? West to east?\"\r\n\r\nTiffani exhaled, something tight in her chest loosening just slightly. Not her tools. Not her mind. The world had bent, and someone else had seen it too.\r\n\r\n\"Why is it doing that?\" Aedric asked, and he sounded young, suddenly, younger than his casting.\r\n\r\nTiffani didn't answer. She was already moving, crossing to the northern sill where her logbook sat, flipping it open to the last page of blank entries. She wrote the time, the date, the angle. Then she added a single line at the bottom, underlined twice: *Horizon warp confirmed. Ground displacement likely. Cause unknown.*\r\n\r\nCause unknown. The two most dangerous words in engineering.\r\n\r\nAedric was watching her, hands twisted together. \"Senior Merrow, should we—\"\r\n\r\nThe floor lurched.\r\n\r\nNot hard. Not even enough to knock her off balance. But enough that the plumb-line swayed, the spirit-bubble slid left, and the vernier rattled in its housing. Tiffani grabbed the edge of the table, knuckles white, and counted. One breath. Two. The floor steadied. The plumb-line stilled.\r\n\r\nAedric's face had gone pale. \"What was that?\"\r\n\r\nTiffani opened her mouth to answer—seismic shift, minor, probably localized—and then the sound started.\r\n\r\nIt wasn't loud. Not at first. It was a hum, low and resonant, the kind that lived in your bones before your ears. Like someone had struck the world with a mallet and the whole planet was ringing. Tiffani felt it in her teeth, in her ribs, in the soles of her feet. The hum deepened. Grew. Became a roar that had no direction, no source, just weight.\r\n\r\nAedric grabbed the doorframe. \"Senior—\"\r\n\r\nThe air outside the window split open.\r\n\r\nTiffani turned, hands still on the table, and saw it: a shape against the sky, so large her mind refused to give it scale. Dark. Molten at the edges. Falling. No—not falling. Descending. Impossibly fast and impossibly controlled, a column of fused glass and iron and something that glowed orange-white like a forge gone wrong. It cut through the clouds as if they weren't there, trailing light and heat and a sound that wasn't sound, a pressure that made her ears pop.\r\n\r\nThe roar became a scream.\r\n\r\nTiffani couldn't move. Couldn't breathe. Her hands were locked on the table, her eyes locked on the shape, and somewhere in the back of her mind—the part that still worked, still measured, still tried to make sense of the senseless—she thought *impact trajectory, eastern hemisphere, velocity incalculable.*\r\n\r\nAedric was shouting. She couldn't hear him over the roar. He grabbed her arm, pulling, but she couldn't move. The thing in the sky was a spear. A hammer. A judgment. And it was falling, and the world was bending, and every number she'd ever trusted was about to become meaningless.\r\n\r\nThe observatory trembled. Glass cracked. The theodolite toppled, brass ringing on stone.\r\n\r\nTiffani thought, absurdly, *I should have recalibrated.*\r\n\r\nThen the world shattered, and she stopped thinking at all.\r\n\r\n***\r\n\r\n## **CHAPTER 3: THE PIERCING**\r\n\r\nThe world ended in glass and fire.\r\n\r\nJhace didn't remember falling. One breath he was standing at Junction 2–10, hand pressed to the riser, watching the spear descend. The next he was on his back, ears ringing, lungs empty, staring up at a sky that had cracked open like an eggshell. The roar was everywhere—in his bones, in his teeth, in the iron lattice that shrieked and buckled around him. He tried to breathe and couldn't. Tried to move and couldn't. The weight of the world pressed down, flattening him into the stone.\r\n\r\nThen the weight lifted, and he gasped.\r\n\r\nSound returned in pieces. Screaming. Metal tearing. Stone grinding against stone with a sound like the planet chewing itself. Jhace rolled onto his side, coughing, and saw Junction 2–10 in ruins. The riser he'd been touching was split clean through, steam venting in white plumes. The proof-wall behind it had sheared at the base, toppling forward in a slow, terrible arc. Torch-poles lay scattered like kindling. And everywhere—everywhere—the lattice was breaking.\r\n\r\nJhace forced himself to his knees. His hands were shaking. No—the ground was shaking. A tremor, low and constant, like Te'Oga's heartbeat had gone arrhythmic. He grabbed his tool belt, still miraculously strapped to his hip, and stood. His legs held. Barely.\r\n\r\n\"Bram!\" His voice cracked. He tried again, louder. \"Chloen!\"\r\n\r\nNo answer. Just screaming, distant and directionless, and the grind of the world breaking.\r\n\r\nHe started moving. North, toward Junction 2–9, toward where he'd left them. The catwalk beneath his boots was bent, half its support struts sheared away, but it held. He didn't let himself think about what would happen if it didn't. He just moved, one boot in front of the other, hands out for balance, tool belt clanging against his thigh.\r\n\r\nThe air tasted wrong. Copper and ash and something sharper, something chemical that burned the back of his throat. Above him the sky was the color of a bruise, purple-black and veined with light that wasn't lightning. The spear—the thing—had punched through somewhere to the east, and the whole world tilted in its wake. Jhace could feel it: a wrongness in his gut, a sense that down wasn't quite where it used to be.\r\n\r\nJunction 2–9 was worse. The marry point where he'd tightened the bolt—where he'd chalked his mark, where everything had been fine—was gone. Just gone. The riser had split lengthwise, peeled open like rotten fruit, and the clamp-ring he'd torqued tight was embedded in the proof-wall fifteen strides away. Bram's tool satchel lay in the rubble, leather scorched black.\r\n\r\nJhace's chest went cold.\r\n\r\n\"Bram!\"\r\n\r\nMovement. A hand, reaching up from behind a toppled beam. Jhace vaulted the riser and dropped to his knees beside it. Bram was pinned from the waist down, a section of catwalk railing across his legs. His face was gray, slick with sweat, but his eyes were open.\r\n\r\n\"Jhace.\" Bram's voice was thin. \"You're alive.\"\r\n\r\n\"So are you.\" Jhace grabbed the railing and pulled. It didn't budge. He set his boots and pulled harder, muscles screaming, but the metal was wedged under a fallen strut. \"Hold on. I'm getting you out.\"\r\n\r\n\"Chloen,\" Bram said. \"She ran. Toward the Crown. Find her.\"\r\n\r\n\"I'm not leaving you.\"\r\n\r\n\"Jhace.\" Bram's hand closed around his wrist, grip weak but steady. \"Find her. I'm not going anywhere.\"\r\n\r\nJhace stared at him, jaw tight, then nodded once. He pulled off his jacket and wadded it under Bram's head. \"I'll be back.\"\r\n\r\n\"I know.\"\r\n\r\nJhace ran.\r\n\r\nThe Underface was a maze of collapsed catwalks and broken risers, every junction a new disaster. He climbed over rubble, squeezed through gaps in the lattice, followed the screaming north. Twice the ground shook hard enough to knock him off his feet. Once a proof-wall groaned and dropped another layer of stone, missing him by three strides. He didn't stop. Couldn't stop. Chloen was out here somewhere, and if she'd run toward the Crown—toward the glass-houses, toward the tallest structures in the city—she'd run straight into the worst of it.\r\n\r\nHe found her at the base of the Glass Quarter access ramp, or what was left of it. The ramp had sheared away from its moorings, twisted into a corkscrew of iron and stone. And at the bottom, pinned beneath a section of shattered observatory wall, was Chloen.\r\n\r\nShe wasn't moving.\r\n\r\nJhace slid down the rubble pile and dropped beside her. \"Chloen. Chloen, look at me.\"\r\n\r\nHer eyes fluttered open. Blood ran from a gash across her forehead, matting her hair, and her breathing was shallow and fast. \"Jhace?\"\r\n\r\n\"I'm here. I've got you.\" He grabbed the edge of the wall section and heaved. It shifted a finger-width, then caught. He tried again, bracing his boots against the ground, and the stone scraped aside. Chloen gasped, body curling inward, and Jhace saw the blood.\r\n\r\nThere was so much blood.\r\n\r\nHer side was torn open, ribs visible through shredded cloth and skin. Her left leg bent wrong below the knee. Her hands were clutching at the wound, trying to hold herself together, and her eyes were wide and terrified.\r\n\r\n\"I can't—\" Her voice broke. \"Jhace, I can't—\"\r\n\r\n\"You're fine. You're going to be fine.\" He pulled off his shirt, wadded it up, and pressed it to her side. She screamed. He kept pressing, counting breaths, trying to remember what his father had taught him about field dressings, about keeping pressure, about—\r\n\r\nShe was going to die.\r\n\r\nThe thought hit him like a fist. She was going to die here, in the rubble, bleeding out faster than he could stop it, and he'd be the one who'd have to tell Bram, the one who'd have to carry her body back, the one who'd failed again—\r\n\r\nNo.\r\n\r\nSomething inside him snapped. Not broke—snapped into place, like a bolt catching threads. Heat flooded his chest, his arms, his hands where they pressed against Chloen's side. Not the heat of fever or forge-work, but something warmer, something that pulsed in time with his heartbeat. He felt it move through him like a current, pouring out of his palms and into her, and suddenly he could see the wound. Not with his eyes—with something else. He could see the torn muscle, the cracked ribs, the severed vessels pumping blood into nothing. He could see where things were supposed to connect, where they'd come apart.\r\n\r\nAnd he could fix it.\r\n\r\nHe didn't know how. Didn't question it. He just pressed his hands tighter and let the heat pour through. Copper-gold light bloomed beneath his palms, warm as a banked forge, and Chloen gasped. The wound beneath his hands began to close. Slowly, impossibly, the torn edges knit back together. Muscle reconnected. Bone straightened. Skin sealed over raw flesh, leaving only a pale line where the gash had been.\r\n\r\nChloen stopped screaming. Her breathing evened. The blood stopped.\r\n\r\nJhace lifted his hands, staring. His palms glowed faintly, copper-gold veins tracing across the skin like he'd dipped them in molten metal. Then the light faded, and he was just staring at his own hands, unmarked except for the old calluses and—\r\n\r\nThe burn.\r\n\r\nHis right palm was scorched, a copper-gold mark the size of a coin, perfectly circular. It didn't hurt. It should have hurt. But all he felt was exhaustion, sudden and crushing, like someone had pulled every ounce of strength out of him and left only the hollow.\r\n\r\n\"Jhace?\" Chloen's voice was small. \"What did you do?\"\r\n\r\nHe opened his mouth. Closed it. \"I don't know.\"\r\n\r\nA sound behind him—rubble shifting, boots scraping stone. Jhace turned, one hand dropping to his tool belt, and froze.\r\n\r\nA woman stood at the top of the rubble pile. Thin, soot-streaked, one leg bent at an angle that meant broken. Her hair was pinned up with brass clips, half of them torn loose, and her eyes—pale gray, sharp as cut glass—were locked on Jhace's hands.\r\n\r\nShe took a step, stumbled, and Jhace moved without thinking. He caught her before she hit the ground, one arm around her waist, and felt her weight sag against him. She was lighter than she looked. Shaking.\r\n\r\n\"Your hand,\" she said, voice hoarse. \"It's—you were—\"\r\n\r\n\"Are you hurt?\" Jhace asked.\r\n\r\nShe laughed, a thin, brittle sound. \"Am I hurt. Yes. I'm hurt.\" She shifted her weight off the broken leg and hissed through her teeth. \"Observatory collapsed. I was—there was glass—\"\r\n\r\nShe didn't finish. Jhace looked past her, up the rubble pile, and saw it: the remains of the glass-house observatory, a skeletal framework of iron and shattered panes. And beneath one of the fallen beams, still glowing cherry-hot from the impact, a section of molten glass pooled and cooled.\r\n\r\n\"Can you walk?\" he asked.\r\n\r\n\"No.\"\r\n\r\n\"Then hold on.\"\r\n\r\nHe shifted his grip, got one arm under her knees, and lifted. She was all sharp edges and tension, body rigid in his arms, but she didn't fight him. He carried her down the rubble pile, past Chloen—who was sitting up now, staring at her own side like she didn't believe it—and set the woman down on a flat section of broken catwalk.\r\n\r\n\"Stay here,\" Jhace said. \"I need to check—\"\r\n\r\nThe ground lurched.\r\n\r\nNot a tremor. A lurch, like Te'Oga had tripped over its own axis. The rubble pile shifted, cascading, and the iron framework of the observatory groaned. Jhace grabbed the woman's arm, pulling her back, and looked up just in time to see the beam fall.\r\n\r\nIt was massive. Twelve rods long, iron cored with molten glass still dripping from the joints, falling straight toward them. Jhace didn't think. He lunged forward, planted his boots, and caught it.\r\n\r\nThe impact drove him to his knees. The beam was heavy—impossibly heavy—and hot enough that he felt his gloves start to smoke. His palms burned. His arms screamed. But he held it, angled just enough that the molten glass dripped past them instead of onto them, and for five breaths he was the only thing standing between this woman and a death by fire.\r\n\r\nThen hands grabbed his shoulders—Chloen, on her feet somehow, pulling him back—and the beam crashed down where they'd been standing.\r\n\r\nJhace collapsed onto his back, breath gone, staring up at the bruised sky. His hands were on fire. Not the warm heat from before—actual fire, searing pain that climbed from his palms to his wrists. He tried to flex his fingers and couldn't. Tried to sit up and failed.\r\n\r\nA face appeared above him. The woman. Her gray eyes were wide, pupils blown, and her hands hovered over his chest like she didn't know where to touch.\r\n\r\n\"You just—\" She stopped. Started again. \"You caught it. You caught a falling beam with your bare hands and you're not dead.\"\r\n\r\nJhace tried to answer. His mouth wouldn't work.\r\n\r\n\"Jhace,\" Chloen said, somewhere to his left. \"Jhace, your hands.\"\r\n\r\nHe turned his head, slow as rust, and looked.\r\n\r\nHis gloves were gone. Burned away, maybe, or he'd lost them in the chaos. His palms were raw, blistered, the copper-gold mark on his right hand now ringed with an angry red burn. And running up his right forearm, stark against the soot and ash, was a line. Thin. Bright. Copper-gold, like someone had drawn it with a hot stylus. It didn't hurt. Not yet. But he could feel it, a faint warmth beneath the skin, pulsing in time with his heartbeat.\r\n\r\n\"What did you just do?\" the woman asked again, quieter this time.\r\n\r\nJhace met her eyes. Gray as storm clouds. Terrified. Alive.\r\n\r\n\"I don't know,\" he said.\r\n\r\nThen the world went gray at the edges, and he stopped knowing anything at all.\r\n\r\n***\r\n\r\nWhen he woke, the sky was darker. Not night-dark—wrong-dark, like something was standing between Te'Oga and the sun. Jhace blinked, trying to orient himself, and realized he was still lying on the catwalk. His head was pillowed on something soft—a jacket, maybe—and someone had wrapped his hands in strips of cloth that smelled like oil and copper.\r\n\r\nHe tried to sit up. A hand pressed against his chest, firm but not rough.\r\n\r\n\"Don't,\" the woman said. \"You passed out. Give it a minute.\"\r\n\r\nJhace turned his head. She was sitting beside him, back against a section of broken railing, her broken leg splinted with what looked like pieces of a plumb-line rod and torn fabric. Her face was cleaner now, the soot wiped away in streaks, and he could see the bruise forming along her jaw. She looked exhausted. Looked like she'd been watching him for a while.\r\n\r\n\"Chloen?\" Jhace asked, voice rough.\r\n\r\n\"She's fine. Better than fine. She's—\" The woman stopped, shook her head. \"She shouldn't be walking. That wound should have killed her. But she's over there, helping pull people out of the rubble.\"\r\n\r\nJhace followed her gaze and saw Chloen thirty strides away, lifting a beam off a trapped man's legs with two other survivors. Her movements were careful, precise, but she was upright. Moving. Alive.\r\n\r\n\"You did that,\" the woman said. \"I watched you. Your hands glowed, and the wound closed, and she just—got up. Like it never happened.\"\r\n\r\nJhace looked at his wrapped hands. \"I don't know what I did.\"\r\n\r\n\"Neither do I.\" The woman leaned her head back against the railing, eyes on the bruised sky. \"But I measured the horizon this morning. Four times. It was warped. Three arc-spans, west to east. Impossible. And then that thing fell—\" She gestured vaguely upward. \"—and the world broke, and now you're healing people with your hands.\"\r\n\r\nJhace stared at her. \"You measured the horizon?\"\r\n\r\n\"I'm a glass-guild engineer. I measure things.\" She looked at him, and for the first time since he'd caught her, something like a smile tugged at the corner of her mouth. \"Tiffani Merrow. Senior in the Structural division. Or I was, before my observatory became rubble.\"\r\n\r\n\"Jhace Torrins. Heat-grid maintenance.\"\r\n\r\n\"I know. Your apprentice told me.\" Tiffani tilted her head, studying him. \"She also said you saved her life. Twice. Once with the—\" She gestured at his hands. \"—and once by catching a beam that should have crushed both of us.\"\r\n\r\nJhace didn't answer. He looked at his hands again, at the copper-gold line running up his forearm, and thought about the heat that had poured through him. About the way he'd seen Chloen's wound, not with his eyes but with something deeper. About the certainty he'd felt, in that moment, that he could fix it.\r\n\r\n\"I should check on Bram,\" he said.\r\n\r\n\"Your mentor.\" Tiffani nodded. \"Chloen told me. He's pinned at Junction 2–9. We'll go together.\"\r\n\r\n\"You can't walk.\"\r\n\r\n\"Then you'll carry me.\" She said it flat, matter-of-fact, like it was the only logical solution. \"You're good at that.\"\r\n\r\nJhace almost laughed. Would have, if his ribs didn't hurt and his hands weren't burning and the world hadn't just ended. Instead he said, \"Yeah. I'll carry you.\"\r\n\r\nHe got to his feet, slow and unsteady, and offered her his wrapped hand. She took it, her grip strong despite the tremor, and he pulled her up. She balanced on one leg, leaning into him, and for a breath they just stood there, soot-streaked and bleeding and alive, staring at each other over the ruins of the world.\r\n\r\n\"What did you just do?\" Tiffani asked, one more time.\r\n\r\nJhace looked at his hands. At the copper-gold line. At the woman who'd asked the same question three times now and still hadn't looked away.\r\n\r\n\"I don't know,\" he said. \"But I think everything just changed.\"\r\n\r\nTiffani's eyes held his, storm-gray and unblinking. \"I think you're right.\"\r\n\r\nBehind them, Chloen called his name. The rubble shifted.
