The world groaned, broken but not dead, and Jhace Torrins—hands wrapped, heart hammering, something new and impossible burning beneath his skin—turned toward the sound of people who needed him.\r\n\r\nHe didn't let go of Tiffani's hand.\r\n\r\nShe didn't ask him to.\r\n\r\n***\r\nHere's **Part 2** (Chapters 4–5 + Interlude I):\r\n\r\n---\r\n\r\n## **CHAPTER 4: BREATH CATCH**\r\n\r\nThree tides after the world broke, Jhace woke to the sound of hammering.\r\n\r\nHe lay still, listening. Bram's rhythm. Steady. Alive. He opened his eyes to the soot-blackened ceiling of the forge recovery room and flexed his hands. The bandages were fresh. Beneath them his palms throbbed, and in the center of his right palm, the copper-gold mark burned warm.\r\n\r\nThe door opened. Bram filled the frame, soot-streaked and older-looking, one arm in a sling.\r\n\r\n\"You're awake,\" Bram said.\r\n\r\n\"How long?\"\r\n\r\n\"A tide and a half.\" Bram sat on the bench opposite. \"Chloen said you healed her. Said your hands glowed.\"\r\n\r\nJhace unwrapped his right hand. The mark stared back at him, copper-gold and circular, the size of a coin. It didn't hurt. It should have hurt.\r\n\r\nBram studied it without touching. \"It's not going away.\"\r\n\r\n\"I know.\"\r\n\r\nFootsteps in the doorway. Jhace looked up. Tiffani stood there, soot-streaked, leg splinted, carrying something wrapped in cloth. She crossed the room without asking and sat beside Bram.\r\n\r\n\"You're awake,\" she said.\r\n\r\n\"So I'm told.\"\r\n\r\nShe unwrapped the cloth and set a piece of metal on the bench between them. A flange, bent at a stress fracture. \"I've been studying this. It should have snapped clean through during the impact. But it didn't.\" She picked it up, held it in both hands. \"Something held it.\"\r\n\r\nHer fingers pressed to the metal. Blue-silver light shimmered across the surface, faint as moonlight. The flange straightened slowly, the bend smoothing out like the metal remembered what it was supposed to be. The shimmer faded. She set it down, flat and whole.\r\n\r\nJhace stared. \"You did that.\"\r\n\r\n\"Yes.\" She rolled up her sleeve. A faint blue-silver patina traced the inside of her wrist, like frost on glass. \"And it costs.\"\r\n\r\nBram leaned forward, studying her wrist with the same careful attention he'd given Jhace's palm. \"You're both carrying it now.\"\r\n\r\nTiffani looked at Jhace. \"You forced it. When you healed your apprentice. I watched you. You just poured everything into her, no control, no structure. It's a miracle you didn't burn yourself out completely.\"\r\n\r\nJhace felt his jaw tighten. \"I didn't know what I was doing.\"\r\n\r\n\"I know. But I do.\" She held out the flange. \"I think there are patterns. Rules. Not the kind that control you—the kind that keep you alive.\"\r\n\r\n\"You want to teach me your way.\"\r\n\r\n\"I want to teach you not to die.\" She stood, slow and careful, favoring her splinted leg. \"You heal. I shape. They're different, but they're not opposite. I think we can help each other. If you're willing.\"\r\n\r\nJhace looked at Bram. Bram shrugged. \"Your choice. But she's right. You're good with metal. Less good with staying alive.\"\r\n\r\nJhace turned back to Tiffani. She was watching him, patient and precise, like she had all the time in the world and none of it to waste.\r\n\r\nHe thought about the heat that had poured through him when he'd healed Chloen. About the way it had felt right and terrifying and utterly beyond his control. About collapsing in the rubble and not getting back up.\r\n\r\n\"Alright,\" Jhace said. \"Teach me.\"\r\n\r\nTiffani's expression didn't change, but something in her posture eased. \"Good. We start tomorrow.\"\r\n\r\nShe limped toward the door, then stopped. Turned back. \"Why did you pull me out of the rubble? You didn't know me.\"\r\n\r\nJhace looked at his wrapped hands. \"Metal doesn't leave. But people do. I'm tired of letting them.\"\r\n\r\nTiffani was quiet for five breaths. Then she said, \"Good answer.\"\r\n\r\nShe left.\r\n\r\nBram stood, crossing to the forge. \"About time you accepted help.\"\r\n\r\nJhace almost smiled. \"Took me long enough.\"\r\n\r\n\"Better late than never.\" Bram gestured toward the main room. \"Come on. Chloen's been asking about you.\"\r\n\r\nJhace stood. His hands still hurt. His chest still ached. But something had shifted. Something had caught, like a bolt threading home.\r\n\r\nOutside, the city was still broken. The sky was still bruised. But somewhere in the ruins, Tiffani Merrow was measuring fractures and learning to mend them.\r\n\r\nAnd Jhace Torrins, hands glowing faint copper-gold beneath the bandages, walked into the forge and started listening.\r\n\r\n***\r\n\r\n## **CHAPTER 5: FIRST FIX**\r\n\r\nJunction 7 sat two cables east of the Underface, where the heat-grid bent north toward the Outer Crown. Five tides after the Piercing, the marry point was dying.\r\n\r\nJhace pressed his palm to the riser and felt the wrongness immediately. The metal was cold—not hand-warm the way it should be, but cold enough to pull heat from his skin. The hum underneath was fractured, layered with harmonics that clashed instead of blending. Someone had tried to weld the split seam shut without relieving the stress, and now the whole junction was fighting itself.\r\n\r\n\"This is bad,\" Jhace said.\r\n\r\nTiffani crouched beside him, one hand on the proof-wall for balance. Her splint was gone, replaced by a tight wrap that let her move but not forget. She ran her fingers along the weld line, eyes narrowed.\r\n\r\n\"Worse than bad,\" she said. \"The load's distributed wrong. If this fails, it'll take the secondary trunk with it. That's thirty risers.\"\r\n\r\n\"Can we fix it?\"\r\n\r\n\"Separately? No.\" She looked at him. \"Together? Maybe.\"\r\n\r\nJhace nodded. They'd been working like this for three tides now—Tiffani teaching, Jhace learning, both of them finding the edges of what they could do. But this was different. This wasn't practice on salvaged flanges in Bram's forge. This was live infrastructure, still carrying heat, still under load. If they failed, people would freeze.\r\n\r\n\"What do you need from me?\" Jhace asked.\r\n\r\n\"Seal the fractures first. I can't reinforce the geometry if the metal's still coming apart.\" Tiffani pulled a piece of chalk from her satchel and marked three spots along the seam. \"Here, here, and here. Just mend the breaks. Don't try to move the metal—that's my job.\"\r\n\r\nJhace studied the marks. \"And if I mess it up?\"\r\n\r\n\"You won't.\"\r\n\r\n\"How do you know?\"\r\n\r\nTiffani looked at him, storm-gray eyes steady. \"Because I'm watching.\"\r\n\r\nSomething in Jhace's chest loosened. He pulled off his gloves and pressed his bare palms to the first mark.\r\n\r\nThe heat came slowly this time. Not a flood, the way it had been with Chloen, but a measured flow. He felt the fracture beneath his hands—a hairline split running three fingers deep into the metal, invisible from the outside but screaming in the grain. He let the warmth seep into it, following the break, convincing the metal to remember what it had been before the stress tore it apart.\r\n\r\nThe copper-gold glow pulsed faint beneath his skin. The fracture sealed. Not perfectly—he could still feel the scar where it had split—but solid. Held.\r\n\r\nHe moved to the second mark. Then the third. By the time he lifted his hands, his palms were shaking and the copper-gold line on his forearm felt hot enough to glow through the bandage.\r\n\r\n\"Done,\" he said.\r\n\r\nTiffani didn't answer. She was already moving, both hands pressed to the riser, blue-silver light shimmering across the metal. Jhace watched her work. Her movements were deliberate, precise, like she was coaxing the metal instead of commanding it. The weld line straightened. The stress points eased. The riser remembered its shape and settled back into true.\r\n\r\nWhen she lifted her hands, the marry point was singing again. Not perfect—there was still a faint third-note underneath, a memory of the break—but stable. Whole.\r\n\r\nTiffani sat back on her heels, breathing hard. Her wrists were visibly stiff, fingers moving slow like cold hinges.\r\n\r\n\"That was—\" Jhace started.\r\n\r\n\"Effective,\" Tiffani finished. She looked at him, and for the first time since they'd started working together, she smiled. Small, brief, but real.\r\n\r\nJhace grinned back. \"Yeah. Effective.\"\r\n\r\nThey sat in silence for five breaths, just listening to the hum. The riser was warm now, heat flowing clean through the junction, and the fractured harmonics had smoothed into something that almost sounded like music.\r\n\r\nFrom behind them, Bram's voice: \"Those two are going to change everything.\"\r\n\r\nJhace turned. Bram stood twenty strides back, tool satchel over one shoulder, watching them with an expression Jhace couldn't quite read.\r\n\r\n\"Or destroy themselves trying,\" Bram added.\r\n\r\nTiffani stood, slow and careful, and brushed chalk dust off her trousers. \"We're not destroyed yet.\"\r\n\r\n\"Give it time,\" Bram said. But he was smiling.\r\n\r\n***\r\n\r\n## **INTERLUDE I: LIGHTING THE FURNACE**\r\n\r\n### **CORLEXI: PERFECT LINES**\r\n\r\nMaster Architect Corlexi pressed his fingertips to the cracked arch and felt the failure.\r\n\r\nNot with his hands—though his hands were there, pale and trembling against the scorched stone. With something deeper. A sense that told him exactly where the load had shifted wrong, where the keystone had split, where the whole structure was three breaths from collapsing and taking forty people with it.\r\n\r\nThe Prism City guildhall was ancient. Twelve rods tall, built in the fourth Smelting when architects still believed geometry alone could hold the world together. Before the Piercing, it had stood perfect. After, the northern wall had buckled, the support columns had cracked, and now the arch—the central arch, the one that held up everything else—was dying.\r\n\r\nCorlexi could fix it.\r\n\r\nHe didn't ask permission. Didn't explain. He just closed his eyes and let the light come.\r\n\r\nBlue-silver. Cold as winter glass. It poured from his fingertips into the stone, tracing the fracture lines, finding every place the arch had come apart. He saw the structure in his mind—not as it was, broken and failing, but as it should be. Perfect angles. Perfect load distribution. Perfect.\r\n\r\nThe stone shifted under his hands. Slowly. The cracks sealed. The keystone straightened. The arch remembered what it was supposed to be and bent back into true.\r\n\r\nBehind him, apprentices gasped. Someone whispered his name like a prayer.\r\n\r\nCorlexi didn't hear them. He was counting angles, calculating tolerances, measuring the curve of the arch against the ideal stored in his mind. Three degrees off. He adjusted. The light pulsed brighter. The stone groaned, then settled.\r\n\r\nPerfect.\r\n\r\nHe lifted his hands and staggered back. His legs wouldn't hold. Someone caught him—Meris, maybe, or one of the junior masters—and lowered him to the ground. He leaned against the wall, breath shallow, and looked at his hands.\r\n\r\nThe blue-silver patina had spread. It covered his knuckles now, crawling up toward his wrists like frost on a window. When he flexed his fingers, they moved slow and stiff, joints grinding like rusted hinges.\r\n\r\n\"Master Corlexi,\" Meris said, kneeling beside him. \"You shouldn't—you need to rest—\"\r\n\r\n\"It's perfect now,\" Corlexi said. His voice sounded distant, hollow. \"The arch is perfect.\"\r\n\r\n\"You're not.\"\r\n\r\nCorlexi looked at the arch. At the flawless curve, the seamless stone, the structure that would stand for another three Smeltings because he'd made it perfect. He thought about the cost—the ache in his bones, the stiffness in his hands, the way his body felt heavier every time he used the light.\r\n\r\n\"Perfection requires sacrifice,\" he said.\r\n\r\nMeris didn't answer. Just helped him stand, shoulders braced under Corlexi's weight, and guided him toward the recovery room.\r\n\r\nCorlexi looked back once. The arch stood perfect. Unbreakable.\r\n\r\nWorth it.\r\n\r\n***\r\n\r\n### **MOBEL: CLEANSING FIRE**\r\n\r\nThe girl screamed when Mobel touched her.\r\n\r\nHe expected it. They always screamed at first. Pain was the proof of purification—the fire that burned away contamination and left only clean flesh behind. The girl's leg was infected, blackened from ankle to knee where the wound had festered. In another tide, maybe two, the rot would reach her blood and she'd be gone.\r\n\r\nBut Mobel could save her.\r\n\r\nHe pressed his palm to her shin, just above the blackened skin, and let the flame come.\r\n\r\nWhite-gold. Hot as molten glass. It poured from his hand into her flesh, seeking the infection, burning it away layer by layer. He felt it resist—felt the contamination fight back, clinging to healthy tissue like rust on iron. But the flame was stronger. It always was.\r\n\r\nThe girl's screams turned to sobs. Mobel didn't flinch. He kept his hand steady, kept the flame flowing, watching the blackened skin peel away and reveal pink flesh beneath. Clean. Pure. Whole.\r\n\r\nWhen he lifted his hand, the leg was healed. Scarred, yes—the fire left marks, always—but the infection was gone. The girl would live.\r\n\r\nShe collapsed, gasping, and her mother pulled her close, weeping gratitude into her hair.\r\n\r\n\"Thank you,\" the mother said, looking up at Mobel with tear-streaked cheeks. \"Thank you, Healer. Thank you.\"\r\n\r\nMobel nodded. He didn't smile. Smiling would have implied pleasure, and there was no pleasure in this. Only necessity.\r\n\r\n\"Purity requires pain,\" he said. \"But pain is temporary. Contamination is forever.\"\r\n\r\nThe mother nodded like she understood. She didn't. None of them did. They came to him desperate, begging for miracles, and he gave them fire. They left grateful, never realizing the cost.\r\n\r\nNot to them. To him.\r\n\r\nMobel looked at his hands. The white-gold glow had faded, but the damage remained. His palms were thin now, the skin translucent enough that he could see veins beneath. His fingertips had gone numb three tides ago. And when he touched his own chest, searching for his heartbeat, he felt nothing.\r\n\r\nNot the beat. Not the warmth. Nothing.\r\n\r\nHe was becoming hollow.\r\n\r\nA voice behind him—Valis, one of the younger acolytes who'd started following him after the Piercing. \"Master Mobel. There are six more waiting. Should I send them in?\"\r\n\r\nMobel looked at his hands. At the thin, translucent skin. At the places where feeling had already fled.\r\n\r\n\"Send them in,\" he said.\r\n\r\nValis hesitated. \"Master, you should rest. You've been working since dawn—\"\r\n\r\n\"Contamination doesn't rest.\" Mobel turned, and Valis stepped back. \"Neither do I.\"\r\n\r\nHe worked through the rest of the tide. Burns. Infections. Poisoned wounds. Each one he touched, each flame he called, left him thinner. Colder. More distant. By the time the last patient left, his hands barely responded when he tried to flex them.\r\n\r\nValis brought him water. Mobel drank without tasting it.\r\n\r\n\"They're calling you a saint,\" Valis said quietly. \"The people. They say you take their suffering and give them purity.\"\r\n\r\n\"I'm not a saint.\"\r\n\r\n\"Then what are you?\"\r\n\r\nMobel looked at his hands. At the thin, numb skin. At the places where he'd burned himself hollow trying to burn others clean.\r\n\r\n\"Necessary,\" he said.\r\n\r\nValis didn't ask again.\r\n\r\nOutside, the sun set over the eastern desert, painting the broken world in shades of red and gold. Mobel stood at the window, watching the light fade, and felt nothing.\r\n\r\nNo warmth. No cold. No pain.\r\n\r\nJust the certainty that tomorrow there would be more patients. More contamination. More fire.\r\n\r\nAnd he would burn it all away, piece by piece, until there was nothing left.\r\n\r\nNot in them.\r\n\r\nNot in him.\r\n\r\nPurity required sacrifice.\r\n\r\nAnd Mobel had always been willing to pay.\r\n\r\n***\r\n\r\n## **CHAPTER 6: TEACHING RHYTHM**\r\n\r\n\r\nTiffani woke to the smell of hot iron and old smoke.\r\n\r\nShe lay still for five breaths, orienting herself. Bram's forge. The guest room—smaller than the recovery room, barely wide enough for the cot and a bench. She'd been staying here for three tides now, ever since the Glass Quarter had become too dangerous to navigate alone with a healing leg.\r\n\r\nHer wrists ached. She flexed her fingers, slow and deliberate, feeling the stiffness that had settled in overnight. The blue-silver patina was faint in the dim light, but she could trace it with her eyes—thin lines along the inside of her wrists, creeping toward her palms.\r\n\r\nShe sat up, pulled on her boots, and wrapped her wrists in the cloth strips she kept beside the cot. Not bandages. Just compression, to remind her joints how to move.\r\n\r\nOutside, voices. Jhace and Bram, talking low. She stood, rolled her shoulders, and stepped into the forge.\r\n\r\nJhace sat at the anvil, hands wrapped, holding a piece of bent rebar. He was staring at it like it might tell him something. Bram stood beside him, arms crossed, watching with the expression that meant he was waiting for Jhace to figure it out himself.\r\n\r\nTiffani crossed the room. \"You're up early.\"\r\n\r\nJhace looked up, and something in his expression eased. \"Couldn't sleep.\"\r\n\r\n\"Me neither.\" She gestured at the rebar. \"What are you working on?\"\r\n\r\n\"Trying to.\" He set it down with a dull clang. \"I can feel where it's bent. I can see where it needs to go back. But when I try to move it, it just—resists. Like the metal doesn't trust me.\"\r\n\r\nTiffani picked up the rebar and ran her fingers along the bend. Stress fracture, invisible from the outside, running three fingers deep. Jhace had been trying to heal it without addressing the underlying structure. No wonder it resisted.\r\n\r\n\"You're trying to mend it,\" she said. \"But the geometry's wrong. You can't heal something that doesn't know what shape it's supposed to be.\"\r\n\r\nJhace frowned. \"So what do I do?\"\r\n\r\n\"Let me show you.\" She set the rebar on the anvil and pressed both hands to it. The blue-silver shimmer started slow, tracing the grain, finding the stress points. She didn't force the metal back into shape—just reminded it where true was. The bend eased. The fracture sealed.\r\n\r\nShe lifted her hands and held out the rebar. Straight. Whole.\r\n\r\nJhace took it, turning it over in his hands. \"How do you do that?\"\r\n\r\n\"Practice. And someone taught me.\" She pulled a stool close and sat, favoring her healing leg. \"Sit. I'll teach you.\"\r\n\r\nHe sat.\r\n\r\nFor the next two turns, they worked. Tiffani showed him how to read grain lines, how to feel load distribution, how to guide metal instead of commanding it. She demonstrated each technique—warm glow that mended fractures, cool shimmer that reinforced geometry, bright flash that cut away contamination, soft whisper that eased tension.\r\n\r\nJhace watched her hands like he was memorizing them.\r\n\r\n\"There are four ways to work metal,\" Tiffani said, setting down a warped flange. \"What you do naturally—mending breaks, sealing cracks. That's **Wholeness**.\" She tapped the straightened rebar. \"What I do—geometry, structure, reinforcing angles. That's **Form**.\" She picked up a corroded bolt. \"Burning away contamination, cutting out rot. That's **Purity**.\" She held up a stress-twisted bracket. \"And releasing tension, letting go of what's fighting itself. That's **Entropy**.\"\r\n\r\nJhace stared at her. \"You're naming them.\"\r\n\r\n\"Someone has to.\" Tiffani met his eyes. \"You've been using all four without knowing what they're called. That's dangerous. If you don't understand what you're doing, you can't control it. And if you can't control it—\"\r\n\r\n\"It burns me out,\" Jhace finished quietly.\r\n\r\n\"Yes.\"\r\n\r\nHe looked at his wrapped hands. At the copper-gold mark on his right palm, the vein-line tracing up his forearm. \"So I learn all four?\"\r\n\r\n\"You learn to recognize them. To know when you're using Wholeness and when you're trying to do Form without the vocabulary for it.\" Tiffani picked up another piece—a bolt with stripped threads. \"You don't have to master all four. But you need to understand them. Otherwise you'll try to do everything at once and tear yourself apart.\"\r\n\r\nJhace was quiet for ten breaths. Then he said, \"Show me again.\"\r\n\r\nShe did.\r\n\r\nBy the time they stopped, her wrists were locked and his hands were shaking. But the pile of broken things on the bench was smaller, and the pile of mended things was growing.\r\n\r\n\"How do you know all this already?\" Jhace asked, halfway through the second turn.\r\n\r\nTiffani paused, a piece of warped flange in her hands. She felt the question settle in her chest, heavy and familiar.\r\n\r\n\"I had a good teacher,\" she said. \"Once.\"\r\n\r\n\"What happened to them?\"\r\n\r\nTiffani set the flange down. \"She fell. I miscalculated a load, and she fell.\"\r\n\r\nJhace was quiet for five breaths. Then he said, \"I'm sorry.\"\r\n\r\n\"It was a long time ago.\" Tiffani flexed her fingers, feeling the stiffness creep back. \"But I remember what she taught me. And I'm teaching you.\"\r\n\r\nJhace nodded. He picked up another piece of metal—the bolt with stripped threads—and held it out. \"Show me again.\"\r\n\r\nShe did.\r\n\r\nBy the time they stopped, her wrists were locked and his hands were shaking. But the pile of broken things on the bench was smaller, and the pile of mended things was growing.\r\n\r\nBram appeared in the doorway, holding two tin cups. \"You two planning to eat, or just work till you collapse?\"\r\n\r\nTiffani took a cup. Broth, hot and bitter. She drank it in three swallows.\r\n\r\nJhace set down the bolt he'd been working on and looked at her. \"Thank you. For teaching me.\"\r\n\r\n\"You're a fast learner.\"\r\n\r\n\"You're a good teacher.\"\r\n\r\nTiffani felt something warm settle in her chest. Not the blue-silver shimmer. Something else. Something that didn't have a name yet but felt like trust.\r\n\r\nA knock at the forge door. Bram opened it, and a man stepped through—older, soot-streaked, wearing the insignia of the Guildhall on his shoulder.\r\n\r\n\"I'm looking for Jhace Torrins,\" the man said.\r\n\r\nJhace stood. \"I'm Jhace.\"\r\n\r\nThe man studied him, eyes lingering on his wrapped hands. \"They're calling you a Wholeness resonator. Is it true?\"\r\n\r\nJhace glanced at Tiffani. She nodded once, barely visible.\r\n\r\n\"Yes,\" Jhace said.\r\n\r\nThe man smiled. \"Good. We need people like you. Come to the Guildhall tomorrow. We're organizing.\"\r\n\r\nHe left before Jhace could answer.\r\n\r\nTiffani set down her cup. Her hands were still. Her chest was tight.\r\n\r\n\"Organizing,\" she said quietly. \"That's how it starts.\"\r\n\r\nJhace looked at her. \"How what starts?\"\r\n\r\n\"Control.\" She stood, slow and deliberate. \"They'll want to catalog us. Measure us. Tell us where we can work and who we can help. They'll call it coordination, but it's control.\"\r\n\r\n\"You don't know that.\"\r\n\r\n\"I measure things,\" Tiffani said. \"I know how systems fail.\"\r\n\r\nJhace was quiet for ten breaths. Then he said, \"So what do we do?\"\r\n\r\n\"We keep working. Together. Before they decide we can't.\"\r\n\r\nHe nodded.\r\n\r\nOutside, the city was still broken. The sky was still bruised. But in Bram's forge, two people sat side by side, mending the world one piece at a time, and learning to trust the rhythm between them.\r\n\r\n---\r\n\r\n## **CHAPTER 7: PARALLEL LINES**\r\n\r\nTen tides after the Piercing, Jhace and Tiffani moved like they shared one mind.\r\n\r\nThey stood at the collapsed proof-wall in the Glass Quarter's southern edge, where a support beam had buckled and trapped three families in the rubble. The beam was massive—twelve rods long, iron cored with fused glass from the impact, and hot enough that Jhace could feel the heat from two strides away.\r\n\r\n\"Seals first,\" Tiffani said, already moving.\r\n\r\n\"Then structure,\" Jhace finished.\r\n\r\nThey didn't need to discuss it. Jhace pressed his palms to the stress fractures spiderwebbing across the beam's base while Tiffani positioned herself at the midpoint, hands flat against the iron. Copper-gold warmth poured from Jhace's hands, seeping into the metal, convincing the fractures to close. The beam stopped groaning. Stopped fighting itself.\r\n\r\nTiffani's blue-silver shimmer followed a breath later, reinforcing the geometry, locking the structure back into true. The beam straightened. Held.\r\n\r\nThe families crawled out through the gap. Someone was crying. Someone else was thanking them, words tumbling over each other, but Jhace barely heard. He was already moving to the next site.\r\n\r\nBy mid-turn they'd cleared two more collapses. A shattered window frame in the Underface, where Jhace sealed the glass while Tiffani reshaped the iron mullions. A cracked riser at Junction 4, where Tiffani reinforced the marry point and Jhace mended the welds. They worked in silence, hands moving in patterns they'd learned together, weight shifting in sync.\r\n\r\nAt the third site—a refugee shelter with a sagging roof—Chloen met them at the door.\r\n\r\n\"There's a man inside,\" she said. Her voice was tight. \"Poisoned wound. He's not going to last the turn.\"\r\n\r\nJhace followed her in. The man lay on a cot, leg black from ankle to knee, breathing shallow and fast. Jhace had seen wounds like this before. Contamination, deep in the tissue. It would take precision work—cutting away the rot without damaging healthy flesh, then mending what was left.\r\n\r\nHe knelt beside the cot and reached for the man's leg.\r\n\r\nChloen's hand stopped him. \"Let me.\"\r\n\r\nJhace looked up. Chloen's eyes were steady, her jaw set. She wasn't asking.\r\n\r\n\"Chloen—\"\r\n\r\n\"I can do this.\"\r\n\r\nTiffani appeared in the doorway, watching. She didn't say anything. Just nodded once.\r\n\r\nJhace stepped back.\r\n\r\nChloen pressed both hands to the man's shin. For five breaths, nothing. Then light bloomed beneath her palms—white-gold, bright as molten glass. The man screamed. The contaminated flesh burned away, layer by layer, until only clean tissue remained. Chloen didn't flinch. Didn't stop. And when the white-gold faded, a second light appeared—blue-silver, cool and precise. The structure beneath the wound stabilized. Bone straightened. Muscle reconnected.\r\n\r\nShe lifted her hands. The leg was healed. Scarred, but whole.\r\n\r\nThe man stopped screaming. Started breathing.\r\n\r\nChloen stood, swaying, and Jhace caught her before she fell. Her hands were shaking, the skin faintly translucent where the light had poured through. But her eyes were clear.\r\n\r\n\"Two,\" she whispered. \"I used two.\"\r\n\r\nJhace looked at Tiffani. Tiffani's expression was unreadable.\r\n\r\n\"That's not supposed to be possible,\" Jhace said quietly.\r\n\r\n\"I know,\" Chloen said. She looked at her hands, then at Jhace. \"But I did it anyway.\"\r\n\r\nOutside, the refugees were talking. Jhace heard the words through the shelter walls—\"miracle workers,\" \"dance of repair,\" \"like they're married.\" He felt heat climb his neck.\r\n\r\nTiffani stood beside him, arms crossed, watching the crowd disperse. \"They're talking about us.\"\r\n\r\n\"I noticed.\"\r\n\r\n\"They think we're—\" She stopped. Started again. \"They think we work like we're married.\"\r\n\r\nJhace's mouth went dry. \"We're not.\"\r\n\r\n\"I know.\"\r\n\r\n\"We're just—\" He gestured vaguely at the shelter, at the repaired roof, at the city. \"—working.\"\r\n\r\n\"Right. Working.\"\r\n\r\nThey stood in silence for ten breaths. Neither moved. Neither looked at the other.\r\n\r\nChloen appeared in the doorway, grinning despite her exhaustion. \"You two work like you're married.\"\r\n\r\nJhace and Tiffani both turned, denying it at the same time, words tripping over each other. Chloen's grin widened.\r\n\r\n\"I'm just saying what everyone else is thinking,\" she said, and disappeared back inside.\r\n\r\nJhace felt his face burn. He didn't look at Tiffani. Couldn't. If he looked at her now, she'd see everything—the way his pulse kicked up when she stood close, the way he'd started tracking her movements before she made them, the way he thought about her hands more than the metal they touched.\r\n\r\n\"We should go,\" Tiffani said, voice careful. \"There's another site waiting.\"\r\n\r\n\"Yeah.\"\r\n\r\nThey walked back toward the Underface in silence. The city stretched around them, still broken but less so. Every tide they fixed a little more. Every tide the gaps between them closed a little further.\r\n\r\nBy the time they reached Bram's forge, Jhace's hands were shaking and Tiffani's wrists were locked. They sat side by side on the bench outside, not touching, both staring at the bruised sky.\r\n\r\n\"Your weight shifts just before you move,\" Jhace said quietly. \"Left foot first. Always.\"\r\n\r\nTiffani was silent for five breaths. Then she said, \"You hold your breath when you're concentrating. Three seconds exactly.\"\r\n\r\nJhace turned. She was already looking at him, storm-gray eyes steady and terrified.\r\n\r\n\"We're reading each other,\" he said.\r\n\r\n\"Yes.\"\r\n\r\n\"That's—\"\r\n\r\n\"Dangerous,\" Tiffani finished. \"If one of us slips, the other follows.\"\r\n\r\nJhace thought about the near-Hollowing he'd felt in the back of his mind, the edge he'd almost stepped over when he'd worked too hard, pushed too far. He thought about Tiffani's hands, stiff and aching, moving slower every tide. He thought about what would happen if they kept working like this—burning themselves out in parallel, neither one willing to stop first.\r\n\r\nHe thought about stopping.\r\n\r\nThen he looked at the city, at the heat rising from the Underface, at the people moving through the rubble like ants rebuilding a hill.\r\n\r\n\"We keep going,\" he said.\r\n\r\nTiffani nodded. \"Together.\"\r\n\r\n\"Together.\"\r\n\r\nInside the forge, Bram was hammering something into shape. The rhythm was steady, familiar, the sound of the world being mended one strike at a time.\r\n\r\nJhace flexed his hands. The copper-gold line on his forearm was brighter now, longer, tracing halfway to his elbow. It didn't hurt. Not yet. But he could feel it—a warmth beneath the skin that pulsed in time with his heartbeat.\r\n\r\nBeside him, Tiffani was wrapping her wrists again, movements slow and deliberate. The blue-silver patina had spread to her knuckles, faint but unmistakable.\r\n\r\nThey were both carrying it now. Both paying the cost.\r\n\r\nBut they were still here. Still holding on.\r\n\r\nStill holding each other.\r\n\r\n***\r\n\r\n## **CHAPTER 8: FOUR PRINCIPLES**\r\n\r\nTwelve tides after the Piercing, Jhace stood in front of a crowd and tried not to vomit.\r\n\r\nThe public works yard was packed—fifty people, maybe more, pressed shoulder-to-shoulder around the collapsed catwalk. Salvage workers. Refugees. Guild officials with their brass insignia gleaming in the afternoon light. Someone had spread word that the \"Wholeness resonator\" would demonstrate what he could do. Jhace hadn't asked for the audience. But they'd come anyway.\r\n\r\nTiffani stood beside him, arms crossed, watching the crowd with an expression that meant she didn't trust any of them.\r\n\r\n\"You don't have to do this,\" she said quietly.\r\n\r\n\"People need to see it's possible.\"\r\n\r\n\"People need you alive.\"\r\n\r\nJhace looked at the catwalk. Three support struts were cracked, the marry point was sheared, and the whole structure was sagging under its own weight. A textbook failure—the kind that killed people if you didn't catch it fast. He could fix it. He knew he could.\r\n\r\nHe stepped forward. The crowd quieted.\r\n\r\n\"I'm going to try something,\" Jhace said. His voice carried better than he expected. \"Four different repairs. Watch.\"\r\n\r\nHe pressed his palms to the first cracked strut and let the warmth come. Copper-gold light bloomed beneath his hands, seeping into the fracture, convincing the metal to remember what whole felt like. The crack sealed. The crowd murmured.\r\n\r\nSecond strut. He shifted his stance, changed his grip, and felt for the geometry underneath—the angles, the load paths, the places where structure wanted to hold true. Blue-silver shimmer replaced copper-gold. The strut straightened, locked back into alignment. Tiffani had taught him this. Her way, not his.\r\n\r\nSomeone gasped. Jhace ignored it.\r\n\r\nThird strut. Contamination—rust and corrosion eating through the iron where water had pooled. He scraped it away with his thumb, not physically, but with the bright white-gold flash Chloen had shown him. The metal came clean, gleaming like it had just come out of the forge.\r\n\r\nFourth strut. Stress, invisible but killing the metal slowly. He leaned close, whispered to it the way he'd whispered to dying patients, and felt the gray shimmer respond. The tension eased. The metal relaxed.\r\n\r\nThe catwalk held.\r\n\r\nThe crowd erupted—applause, shouts, someone yelling \"Impossible!\" Jhace lifted his hands and stepped back. His vision swam. His legs didn't want to hold. He felt Tiffani's hand on his elbow, steadying him, but it wasn't enough.\r\n\r\n\"I need—\" he started, then stopped. His stomach twisted.\r\n\r\nHe turned and walked—fast, too fast—toward the edge of the yard. Found an alley. Dropped to his knees and vomited.\r\n\r\nThe world spun. His hands were shaking so hard he couldn't hold them still. The copper-gold line on his forearm felt like it was burning through his skin, and underneath it, deeper, he could hear something. Singing. The grain lines in every piece of metal within ten strides, all humming at once, layering over each other until the sound scraped the inside of his skull.\r\n\r\nFootsteps. Tiffani dropped to a crouch beside him, one hand on his back.\r\n\r\n\"You're showing off,\" she said. Her voice was sharp. \"Stop it.\"\r\n\r\nJhace wiped his mouth with the back of his hand. \"People need to see it's possible.\"\r\n\r\n\"They need to see you alive. Not dying in an alley because you burned yourself out trying to impress them.\"\r\n\r\n\"I didn't—\"\r\n\r\n\"Yes, you did.\" She grabbed his wrist and turned his arm over, exposing the vein-line. It glowed faint copper-gold even now, three fingers longer than it had been that morning. \"Look at this. Look at what you're doing to yourself.\"\r\n\r\nJhace pulled his arm back. \"I'm helping.\"\r\n\r\n\"You're killing yourself. There's a difference.\"\r\n\r\nThey stared at each other. Tiffani's eyes were wide, terrified in a way Jhace had never seen. Her hands were shaking as hard as his.\r\n\r\n\"I can't watch you do this,\" she said quietly. \"I can't watch you burn yourself out because you think you have to prove something.\"\r\n\r\n\"I don't—\"\r\n\r\n\"Yes, you do.\" Her voice cracked. \"You think if you save enough people, fix enough things, work hard enough, you can make up for the ones you lost. But you can't. And you're going to kill yourself trying.\"\r\n\r\nJhace opened his mouth. Closed it. The words sat in his throat like stones.\r\n\r\nTiffani stood, slow and deliberate. \"I'm going back to the forge. You can come with me, or you can stay here and show off until you collapse. Your choice.\"\r\n\r\nShe walked away. Jhace stayed on his knees, hands pressed to the cold stone, listening to the grain-song that wouldn't stop.\r\n\r\nHe counted ten breaths. Twenty. Then he stood and followed her.\r\n\r\n---\r\n\r\nBy the time he reached Bram's forge, pamphlets were already plastered across the city walls.\r\n\r\nJhace stopped at the first one he saw, squinting in the fading light. The text was printed in thick black ink, the letters sharp enough to cut:\r\n\r\n**MIRACLE WORKERS OR MONSTERS?**\r\n\r\n*Who controls the resonance? Who decides who gets healed? The Guildhall demands answers. Public forum, three tides. Attendance mandatory.*\r\n\r\nJhace tore the pamphlet down. Found another ten strides away. Tore that one down too. They kept appearing, pasted to walls and proof-beams and doors, each one asking the same question in the same sharp letters.\r\n\r\nInside the forge, Tiffani sat at the anvil, staring at her hands. The blue-silver patina was darker now, spreading up from her wrists toward her knuckles. When she flexed her fingers, they moved slow and stiff, joints grinding audibly.\r\n\r\nJhace sat beside her. Neither spoke for five breaths.\r\n\r\n\"They're scared,\" Tiffani said finally.\r\n\r\n\"Of us?\"\r\n\r\n\"Of what we can do. Of what it means.\" She looked at him. \"They're going to try to regulate it. Control who uses it, how, where. They'll call it safety. Call it coordination. But it's fear.\"\r\n\r\n\"So what do we do?\"\r\n\r\nTiffani was quiet for ten breaths. Then she said, \"We show up. We answer their questions. And we don't let them separate us.\"\r\n\r\nJhace nodded. \"Together.\"\r\n\r\n\"Together.\"\r\n\r\nOutside, the city was still broken. The pamphlets were still spreading. And somewhere in the Guildhall, people were writing regulations for powers they didn't understand, trying to control something that had already changed the world.\r\n\r\nBut in Bram's forge, two people sat side by side, hands aching and hearts steady, and held on.\r\n\r\n***\r\n\r\n---\r\n\r\n## **CHAPTER 9: ETHICS DEBATE**\r\n\r\nFifteen tides after the Piercing, Tiffani walked into the town hall and felt the trap close around her.\r\n\r\nThe room was packed. Guild officials sat at a long table at the front, brass insignia gleaming on their shoulders, faces stern and unreadable. Behind them, pamphlets plastered the walls—*Miracle Workers or Monsters?* in letters that seemed to get sharper every time Tiffani looked at them. And in the seats, fifty people, maybe more, watching her and Jhace like they were specimens under glass.\r\n\r\nJhace stood beside her, hands wrapped, jaw tight. His tremor was visible even from a distance—a constant shake in his fingers that he couldn't hide anymore. Tiffani wanted to reach for his hand, wanted to anchor him the way he'd anchored her in the rubble. But they were being watched. Judged. And showing weakness now would be a mistake.\r\n\r\nThe lead official—a man named Derent, thin and sharp-eyed—gestured at the chairs in front of the table. \"Sit.\"\r\n\r\nTiffani sat. Jhace followed.\r\n\r\nDerent leaned forward, fingers steepled. \"You've been working together for two anneals now. Repairing infrastructure. Healing the injured. Using what the Guildhall has classified as 'resonance phenomena.'\" He said the words like they tasted bad. \"We need to understand what you're doing. And more importantly, we need to establish guidelines.\"\r\n\r\n\"Guidelines,\" Jhace said. His voice was flat.\r\n\r\n\"Control,\" Tiffani corrected. She met Derent's eyes. \"You want to control who uses this and how. That's what this is about.\"\r\n\r\nDerent didn't flinch. \"We want to ensure public safety. You've demonstrated extraordinary abilities. But without regulation, without oversight, you could just as easily harm people as help them.\"\r\n\r\n\"We haven't harmed anyone.\"\r\n\r\n\"Yet.\" Another official spoke up—a woman with gray hair and cold eyes. \"But what happens when you make a mistake? What happens when you overreach? What happens when someone with your abilities decides they don't need to answer to anyone?\"\r\n\r\nTiffani felt her hands curl into fists. The stiffness in her wrists flared, joints grinding audibly, but she ignored it. \"We're not trying to overthrow anything. We're just trying to keep the city standing.\"\r\n\r\n\"Under whose authority?\" Derent asked. \"The Guildhall's? The city council's? Your own?\"\r\n\r\n\"Under the authority of people dying in the rubble,\" Jhace said. His voice was quiet, but it cut through the room. \"Under the authority of risers failing and proof-walls collapsing and the heat-grid bleeding out while you sit here writing regulations.\"\r\n\r\nThe room went silent.\r\n\r\nDerent's expression didn't change. \"You're emotional. That's understandable. But emotion without structure leads to chaos.\"\r\n\r\n\"And structure without action leads to corpses,\" Tiffani said. She leaned forward. \"You want guidelines? Fine. Here's mine: I fix what's broken. He heals who's hurt. We don't ask permission because people don't have time to wait for your approval.\"\r\n\r\n\"That's exactly the kind of thinking we're trying to prevent,\" the gray-haired woman said. She looked between them, eyes narrowing. \"You two are dangerous. Not because of what you can do—because of how you think. She wants structure.\" She pointed at Tiffani. \"You want freedom.\" She pointed at Jhace. \"That kind of division fractures partnerships. You'll tear each other apart before the year ends.\"\r\n\r\nTiffani felt the words land like a blow. She looked at Jhace. He was already looking at her, storm-gray eyes meeting his brown ones, and for five breaths they just stared at each other.\r\n\r\nThen they both said, at exactly the same time: \"No.\"\r\n\r\nThe word rang through the hall.\r\n\r\nDerent blinked. \"Excuse me?\"\r\n\r\n\"No,\" Jhace said again. He didn't look away from Tiffani. \"We're not dividing. We're not fracturing. We work together.\"\r\n\r\n\"Because together we're stronger,\" Tiffani finished. She turned back to the table. \"You want to regulate us? Go ahead. Write your guidelines. But you're not separating us. We work as a team, or we don't work at all.\"\r\n\r\nThe officials exchanged glances. Whispers rippled through the crowd behind them.\r\n\r\nDerent leaned back in his chair, studying them. \"You realize this refusal could have consequences.\"\r\n\r\n\"We realize,\" Tiffani said. \"Do it anyway.\"\r\n\r\nFor ten breaths, no one spoke. Then Derent sighed and waved them away. \"We'll take your statement under advisement. You're dismissed.\"\r\n\r\nTiffani stood. Her legs were shaking—not from fear, but from exhaustion. She'd been holding herself rigid for so long that her muscles had forgotten how to relax. Jhace stood beside her, and together they walked toward the door.\r\n\r\nBehind them, whispers grew louder. Someone said \"arrogant.\" Someone else said \"necessary.\" Tiffani didn't turn around. She just kept walking, one step at a time, until they were outside in the cold air and the door had closed behind them.\r\n\r\nThey stood in silence for five breaths.\r\n\r\nThen Tiffani felt Jhace's hand slip into hers.\r\n\r\nShe looked down. His fingers were wrapped in cloth, the tremor visible even through the bandages, but his grip was steady. Warm. Real.\r\n\r\nShe squeezed back.\r\n\r\n\"That went well,\" Jhace said.\r\n\r\nTiffani almost laughed. \"They're going to make our lives difficult.\"\r\n\r\n\"They were already doing that.\"\r\n\r\n\"Fair point.\" She looked at him. His face was drawn, exhausted, but his eyes were clear. \"You okay?\"\r\n\r\n\"No.\" He smiled, small and tired. \"But I'm here. And so are you.\"\r\n\r\n\"Together,\" Tiffani said.\r\n\r\n\"Together.\"\r\n\r\nThey walked back toward the Underface, hands still clasped, neither one willing to let go first. The city stretched around them, still broken, still bleeding. But somewhere in the wreckage, two people held on, and that was enough.\r\n\r\nFor now.\r\n\r\n***\r\n\r\n---\r\n\r\n## **CHAPTER 10: FLOW BEND**\r\n\r\nTwo tides after the forum, the debate raged in every corner of the city.\r\n\r\nJhace stood in Bram's forge, staring at a map of the Underface spread across the workbench, and listened to the arguments happening outside. Someone wanted a formal Ethics hearing—three full tides of debate, structured testimony, careful deliberation. Someone else wanted immediate action on Heat-Rib 3, the main conduit that had been failing since the Piercing and now threatened two hundred risers.\r\n\r\nThe choice should have been simple. People were freezing. The grid was dying. But the Guildhall had made it clear: unauthorized repairs would result in sanctions. Possibly arrest.\r\n\r\nTiffani stood on the opposite side of the bench, hands flat on the map, studying the schematic of Heat-Rib 3. Her wrists were wrapped tight, the cloth strips visible beneath her sleeves. She'd stopped trying to hide the stiffness.\r\n\r\n\"They want us to wait,\" she said quietly. \"Three tides for the hearing. Maybe longer if they decide to form a committee.\"\r\n\r\n\"And in three tides, Heat-Rib 3 fails,\" Jhace said. \"Along with the secondary trunk, the junction feeds, and thirty marry points.\"\r\n\r\n\"I know.\"\r\n\r\n\"People die.\"\r\n\r\n\"I know.\" Tiffani looked up, storm-gray eyes tired. \"But if we go without authorization, they'll call it defiance. They'll use it as proof we're dangerous. And next time they won't just threaten—they'll act.\"\r\n\r\nJhace traced the line of Heat-Rib 3 on the map with one wrapped finger. The main artery bent north from the Underface, carrying heat to the Glass Quarter, the Outer Crown, and half the refugee shelters scattered between. If it failed, thousands would freeze before anyone could patch it. If it failed during the night shift, when the temperature dropped, people wouldn't wake up.\r\n\r\n\"I'm going,\" Jhace said.\r\n\r\nTiffani didn't answer for five breaths. Then she said, \"I know.\"\r\n\r\n\"You don't have to come.\"\r\n\r\n\"Yes, I do.\" She straightened, rolling her shoulders. \"You'll kill yourself if I don't.\"\r\n\r\nJhace almost smiled. \"Probably.\"\r\n\r\n\"Then we go together.\" Tiffani pulled a piece of chalk from her satchel and marked three points on the map. \"We'll need to stabilize here, here, and here. The main conduit, the pressure junction, and the overflow valve. If we can get all three holding, the rest should balance.\"\r\n\r\n\"How long?\"\r\n\r\n\"Two turns if we work fast. Three if we're careful.\"\r\n\r\n\"We'll be fast.\"\r\n\r\nTiffani met his eyes. \"Jhace. If we do this—if we make this public—there's no going back. They'll use it against us. They'll say we're reckless. Dangerous. Everything they already think but haven't proven yet.\"\r\n\r\n\"Let them.\" Jhace leaned forward, palms flat on the bench opposite hers. \"I'm tired of waiting for permission to save people.\"\r\n\r\nFor ten breaths, they just stared at each other across the map. The forge was quiet except for the distant ring of Bram's hammer and the low hum of the banked coals. Jhace could see the exhaustion in Tiffani's face, the way her shoulders sagged when she thought no one was looking. He could see the fear, too—not of the work, but of what came after.\r\n\r\n\"Four tides from now,\" Tiffani said finally. \"We make it public. We invite the Guildhall, the city officials, everyone who's been writing pamphlets. We show them what we can do. Proof or catastrophe.\"\r\n\r\n\"Proof or catastrophe,\" Jhace echoed.\r\n\r\nTiffani nodded and turned back to the map, bending close to study the junction points. Jhace moved around the bench to stand beside her, leaning in to see the detail. Their shoulders brushed.\r\n\r\nBoth froze.\r\n\r\nIt wasn't the first time they'd touched. They'd worked side by side for two anneals now, hands overlapping on hot metal, bodies pressed close in tight spaces while they wrestled broken risers back into alignment. But this was different. This wasn't work. This was just them, standing too close in a quiet forge, the map forgotten and the air suddenly too thick to breathe.\r\n\r\nJhace didn't move. Couldn't. If he moved, she'd pull away, and he didn't want her to pull away. He wanted—\r\n\r\nTiffani turned her head. Their faces were a hand-width apart. Close enough that Jhace could see the blue-silver veins tracing across her knuckles, the faint scar along her jaw from the Piercing, the way her pupils widened when she looked at him.\r\n\r\n\"We should plan the approach,\" Jhace said. His voice came out rough.\r\n\r\n\"Yes.\" Tiffani didn't move. \"The approach.\"\r\n\r\nNeither of them looked back at the map.\r\n\r\nJhace's heart was hammering so hard he thought she must be able to hear it. His hands were shaking—not from the tremor, not from exhaustion, but from the effort of not reaching for her. Of not closing the gap. Of not—\r\n\r\n\"Jhace,\" Tiffani said quietly.\r\n\r\n\"Yeah?\"\r\n\r\n\"I—\" She stopped. Started again. \"We should work.\"\r\n\r\n\"Yeah.\"\r\n\r\nBut neither of them moved.\r\n\r\nThe moment stretched. Five breaths. Ten. Jhace felt like the whole world had narrowed to this: the warmth of her shoulder against his, the sound of her breathing, the way she was looking at him like she was trying to solve an equation that didn't have an answer.\r\n\r\nThen Bram's hammer rang loud in the main room, breaking the spell, and they both stepped back at the same time.\r\n\r\nTiffani cleared her throat. \"I'll draft the load calculations. You check the tool inventory.\"\r\n\r\n\"Right. Tools.\"\r\n\r\nShe turned and walked toward the recovery room, movements too careful, too controlled. Jhace stayed at the bench, staring at the map without seeing it, trying to slow his pulse.\r\n\r\nHe'd almost—\r\n\r\nThey'd almost—\r\n\r\nHe pressed his palms flat on the bench and counted breaths until the shaking stopped.\r\n\r\n***\r\n\r\nThey worked through the rest of the tide and into the night. Tiffani drafted calculations, checking load paths and stress tolerances three times each. Jhace cataloged tools, oiled hinges, wrapped handles in fresh cloth. They spoke only when necessary—\"Pass the vernier,\" \"How's the clearance here?\"—but the silence between them wasn't empty. It was full. Heavy. Charged with everything they weren't saying.\r\n\r\nBy the time the torch-poles outside dimmed to embers, the plan was finished. Three sites. Two turns. Proof or catastrophe.\r\n\r\nJhace sat on the bench, flexing his hands. The tremor was worse tonight, a constant shake that wouldn't ease no matter how many breaths he counted. The vein-line on his forearm felt hot beneath the bandage, pulsing faint copper-gold even at rest.\r\n\r\nTiffani sat beside him, close but not touching, wrapping her wrists for the third time that night. Her fingers moved slow and stiff, knuckles grinding audibly with each loop of cloth.\r\n\r\n\"Your hands are getting worse,\" Jhace said quietly.\r\n\r\n\"So are yours.\"\r\n\r\n\"I know.\"\r\n\r\nThey sat in silence for five more breaths.\r\n\r\n\"Jhace,\" Tiffani said. \"When this is over—when we finish the demonstration—what happens to us?\"\r\n\r\nJhace looked at her. She was staring at her wrapped hands, expression unreadable.\r\n\r\n\"What do you mean?\"\r\n\r\n\"The Guildhall's going to regulate us. One way or another. They'll separate us, assign us to different sites, maybe different cities. They'll say it's for efficiency. For safety. But really it's because they're scared of what we can do together.\"\r\n\r\n\"Then we don't let them.\"\r\n\r\n\"How? We can't just—\" She stopped, jaw tight. \"We can't keep defying them forever. Eventually they'll force the issue.\"\r\n\r\nJhace reached over and took her hand. Not the way he had outside the town hall—not tentative, not testing. Just held it, wrapped fingers curling around wrapped fingers, grounding them both.\r\n\r\n\"Then we make them see,\" he said. \"Four tides from now, we show them what happens when we work together. We make it impossible for them to ignore. And we make damn sure they understand that separating us would be the stupidest thing they could do.\"\r\n\r\nTiffani's fingers tightened around his. \"You really think that'll work?\"\r\n\r\n\"No.\" Jhace smiled, tired and honest. \"But I think we have to try anyway.\"\r\n\r\nShe laughed, a soft, broken sound. \"That's a terrible plan.\"\r\n\r\n\"You have a better one?\"\r\n\r\n\"No.\"\r\n\r\nThey sat like that for ten more breaths, hands clasped, shoulders touching, neither one willing to let go first. Outside, the city groaned and shifted, still broken, still bleeding. But inside Bram's forge, two people held on to each other and planned their last, desperate gamble.\r\n\r\nProof or catastrophe.\r\n\r\nTogether.\r\n\r\n***\r\n\r\n---\r\n\r\n## **CHAPTER 11: FRACTURE EDGE**\r\n\r\nEighteen tides after the Piercing, Jhace Torrins tried to save the world by himself.\r\n\r\nThe site was a mess. A mixed steel-and-bone repair in the southern Glass Quarter, where a proof-wall had buckled and crushed three shelters. The survivors had dragged the bodies out already. Now they needed the wall stabilized before the next tremor brought down the rest of the block.\r\n\r\nJhace stood in front of the wall, hands unwrapped, staring at the fracture pattern. It was complicated—stress fractures layered over old welds, contamination eating through the joints, geometry twisted wrong from the impact. The kind of job that needed two people. Three, ideally. And time they didn't have.\r\n\r\nTiffani was delayed. She'd been called away to consult on a different site, a riser failure ten cables west. Jhace had told her he'd wait. But the proof-wall was groaning, and people were watching, and if it came down before she got here—\r\n\r\nHe couldn't wait.\r\n\r\n\"Stand back,\" Jhace said to the refugees huddled nearby.\r\n\r\nThey didn't move fast enough. He raised his voice. \"I said stand back!\"\r\n\r\nThey scattered. Jhace pressed his palms to the proof-wall and let the warmth come.\r\n\r\nThe fractures sealed first. Copper-gold light poured from his hands, seeping into the cracks, convincing the metal to remember wholeness. Easy. He'd done this a hundred times. The wall stopped groaning.\r\n\r\nBut the geometry was still wrong. The stress was still there, invisible but killing the structure slowly. He needed to fix that too.\r\n\r\nHe shifted his grip and called the blue-silver shimmer. Tiffani's way. The structure straightened beneath his hands, angles snapping back into true, load paths realigning. The wall shuddered but held.\r\n\r\nNot done. Contamination at the base—rust eating through the foundation. He scraped it away with a bright white-gold flash, the same way Chloen had shown him. The metal came clean.\r\n\r\nAlmost there. Just one more. The stress, the tension, the places where the wall was fighting itself. He leaned close and whispered, calling the gray shimmer, feeling for the release—\r\n\r\nThe world fractured.\r\n\r\nNot the wall. Him.\r\n\r\nHis mind split down the middle. One half trying to hold the Form, the other trying to maintain the Wholeness, a third piece somewhere screaming about Purity, and a fourth reaching for Entropy and finding nothing. The principles clashed. Fought each other. Tore at him from the inside.\r\n\r\nHe couldn't—\r\n\r\nHe couldn't hold them all.\r\n\r\nThe copper-gold glow flickered. Faded. The warmth in his chest went cold. No—not cold. Hollow. Like someone had reached into his chest and scooped out everything that made him him, leaving only an empty shell.\r\n\r\nJhace tried to remember why he was here. Couldn't. Tried to remember what he was doing. Couldn't. Tried to remember his own name and came up blank.\r\n\r\nHis hands dropped. His knees buckled. The world went gray at the edges, then black, then nothing.\r\n\r\n***\r\n\r\nSomeone was hitting him.\r\n\r\nNot hard. More like tapping. No—slapping. His face stung. The darkness pulled at him, dragged him down, but the slapping wouldn't stop.\r\n\r\n\"Jhace!\" A voice, sharp and terrified. \"Jhace, look at me!\"\r\n\r\nHe couldn't. Couldn't open his eyes. Couldn't move. Couldn't breathe. The hollow in his chest was so vast he thought he might fall into it and never come out.\r\n\r\nHands grabbed his face. Warm. Real. They forced his head up, shook him hard enough that his teeth rattled.\r\n\r\n\"You're here!\" the voice said. Closer now. Familiar. \"I'm here! Look at me! Breathe!\"\r\n\r\nJhace dragged his eyes open. The world swam. Shapes and colors bled together, meaningless, until they finally resolved into—\r\n\r\nGray eyes. Storm-cloud gray. Terrified and furious and so scared she was shaking.\r\n\r\n\"Tiffani,\" he whispered.\r\n\r\n\"Yes.\" Her hands were still on his face, grounding him, anchoring him. \"I'm here. You're here. Breathe.\"\r\n\r\nHe tried. His lungs didn't want to work. He dragged in half a breath, then another, each one feeling like it might be his last.\r\n\r\n\"That's it,\" Tiffani said. Her voice cracked. \"Keep breathing. Stay with me.\"\r\n\r\n\"I couldn't—\" His voice came out broken. \"I couldn't hold it.\"\r\n\r\n\"You don't have to hold everything.\" Tiffani's thumbs pressed against his cheekbones, firm and steady. \"That's what I'm here for.\"\r\n\r\nThe hollow in his chest didn't fill. But it stopped growing. Stopped pulling. Jhace focused on her eyes, on her hands, on the warmth of her skin against his, and slowly—slowly—the world stopped spinning.\r\n\r\nHis hands were ice-cold. Not just cold—frozen, like all the heat had been burned out of them and would never come back. He tried to flex his fingers and they barely moved.\r\n\r\n\"What happened?\" he asked.\r\n\r\n\"You tried to do everything at once.\" Tiffani's voice was shaking as hard as her hands. \"Four principles. Simultaneously. Your mind couldn't handle it. You—\" She stopped, jaw tight. \"You stopped glowing. You just... stopped. And you collapsed.\"\r\n\r\nJhace stared at her. \"I don't remember.\"\r\n\r\n\"I know.\"\r\n\r\n\"I couldn't remember your name.\"\r\n\r\nTiffani's expression broke. For five breaths she just looked at him, eyes wide and wet, and then she pulled him forward and wrapped her arms around him. Not gently. Hard, desperate, like she was trying to hold him together by force.\r\n\r\nJhace buried his face in her shoulder and shook.\r\n\r\nHe didn't cry. Couldn't. The hollow in his chest was too vast for tears. But he shook, and Tiffani held him, and for ten breaths—twenty—they just stayed like that, kneeling in the rubble while the proof-wall groaned and the city carried on around them.\r\n\r\n\"I can't watch you do this,\" Tiffani said finally. Her voice was muffled against his hair. \"I can't watch you burn yourself out because you think you have to save everyone alone.\"\r\n\r\n\"I don't—\"\r\n\r\n\"Yes, you do.\" She pulled back, hands still on his shoulders, and met his eyes. \"You've been doing it since the Piercing. Since before the Piercing. You think if you work hard enough, save enough people, fix enough things, you can make up for the ones you lost. But you can't. And you're going to kill yourself trying.\"\r\n\r\nJhace opened his mouth. Closed it. The words sat in his throat like stones.\r\n\r\n\"You're not alone anymore,\" Tiffani said. \"You have me. You have Bram. You have Chloen. You have people who will hold things with you. But you have to let us.\"\r\n\r\nJhace looked at his hands. They were still ice-cold, still shaking. The copper-gold line on his forearm looked dull, lifeless, like the glow had burned out and would never come back.\r\n\r\n\"What if I can't?\" he whispered.\r\n\r\n\"Then I'll teach you.\" Tiffani's hands tightened on his shoulders. \"The same way you taught me it's okay to trust people. I'll teach you it's okay to let go.\"\r\n\r\nJhace met her eyes. Saw the fear there, the exhaustion, the way she was holding herself together by sheer force of will. And underneath it all, something else. Something warm and steady and unshakeable.\r\n\r\nLove.\r\n\r\nShe loved him. He could see it in every line of her face, every tremor in her hands. She loved him, and she was terrified of losing him, and she was here anyway.\r\n\r\n\"Okay,\" Jhace said. His voice came out broken, but he meant it. \"Teach me.\"\r\n\r\nTiffani exhaled, relief washing over her face. Then she helped him stand, one arm around his waist, supporting his weight. His legs shook but held.\r\n\r\nBehind them, the proof-wall still groaned. Jhace looked at it, at the fractures he'd half-sealed, the geometry he'd half-straightened. It wasn't finished. It wasn't safe.\r\n\r\n\"We need to—\" he started.\r\n\r\n\"No.\" Tiffani's voice was firm. \"We need to get you back to the forge. The wall can wait.\"\r\n\r\n\"But—\"\r\n\r\n\"It can wait.\" She looked at him, storm-gray eyes steady. \"You almost died, Jhace. The wall can wait.\"\r\n\r\nHe wanted to argue. Wanted to pull away, finish the job, prove he could still do this. But his hands were ice-cold and his chest was hollow and Tiffani was holding him like she thought he might disappear if she let go.\r\n\r\nSo he nodded.\r\n\r\nThey walked back to the Underface together, slow and careful, Tiffani supporting half his weight. The city moved around them, people staring, whispering, but Jhace barely noticed. He was too busy trying to remember how to breathe. How to think. How to be Jhace Torrins and not just an empty shell.\r\n\r\nBy the time they reached Bram's forge, the sun had set. Bram took one look at them and didn't ask questions. Just guided Jhace to the recovery room, laid him down on the cot, and covered him with a blanket.\r\n\r\nTiffani sat beside him, hands still wrapped around his, and didn't let go.\r\n\r\n\"I've got you,\" she said quietly.\r\n\r\nJhace closed his eyes. His hands were still ice-cold. The hollow in his chest was still there. But Tiffani was beside him, warm and solid and real, and for now—for now—that was enough.\r\n\r\n***\r\n\r\n---\r\n\r\n## **INTERLUDE II: Rumor and Report**\r\n\r\n### **SALEE: EVERYONE'S PAIN**\r\n\r\nThe woman wouldn't stop screaming.\r\n\r\nSalee pressed her palms to the woman's temples and felt the pain flood in. Not her pain. The woman's. Sharp and jagged and bottomless, a migraine that had lasted six tides and showed no signs of stopping. The kind of pain that made you wish you could die just to make it end.\r\n\r\nSalee could take it.\r\n\r\nShe closed her eyes and let the warmth come—copper-gold light blooming beneath her hands, warmer than a banked forge, warmer than summer sun. The light poured into the woman, seeking the source of the pain, wrapping around it, pulling it out by the roots.\r\n\r\nThe woman's screams faded to whimpers. Then silence. Then a shuddering breath of relief.\r\n\r\nSalee lifted her hands. The woman slumped forward, weeping gratitude into her own lap. Behind her, the line of patients stretched down the Cauldron-Hold clinic hallway and out into the street. Thirty people. Maybe more. All of them hurting. All of them waiting for her.\r\n\r\nSalee stood, swaying, and gestured to the next patient. \"Come.\"\r\n\r\nA man with a shattered knee limped forward. Salee pressed her hands to the joint and felt his pain flood in—sharp bone-deep ache layered over old injuries that had never healed right. She took it. Pulled it into herself, let the warmth carry it away, left him whole and grateful and crying.\r\n\r\nNext patient. A child with fever, skin burning hot enough to blister. Salee took the fever into her own hands, let it bloom across her palms, carried it away into nothing.\r\n\r\nNext patient. A woman with a poisoned wound, blackened flesh eating up her arm. Salee took the rot, took the pain, took everything that hurt and made it hers instead.\r\n\r\nBy the time the sun set, her hands were glowing faintly even at rest. Copper-gold veins traced up her forearms, across her shoulders, down her spine. And when she looked in the mirror at the end of the tide, she barely recognized her own face.\r\n\r\nHer assistant—Kerel, a boy barely old enough to apprentice—brought her water. She drank without tasting it.\r\n\r\n\"You need to rest,\" Kerel said. His voice was careful, the way people spoke to someone they thought might break. \"You've been working since dawn.\"\r\n\r\n\"There are more patients,\" Salee said.\r\n\r\n\"There will always be more patients.\"\r\n\r\n\"Then I'll always work.\"\r\n\r\nKerel set the cup down and knelt beside her chair. \"Healer Salee. When did you last eat?\"\r\n\r\nSalee tried to remember. Couldn't. \"This morning?\"\r\n\r\n\"Three tides ago.\"\r\n\r\nShe looked at her hands. The copper-gold glow pulsed faint beneath the skin, like her veins had forgotten how to carry blood and were carrying light instead. When she pressed her fingers to her own chest, searching for pain—hunger, exhaustion, the bone-deep ache that came from working too hard—she felt nothing.\r\n\r\nNot because the pain wasn't there. Because she couldn't feel it anymore.\r\n\r\nShe'd taken so much pain from others that her own had become invisible. Drowned out. Lost in the flood of everyone else's suffering.\r\n\r\n\"Kerel,\" she said quietly. \"What does my pain feel like?\"\r\n\r\nHe blinked. \"What?\"\r\n\r\n\"My pain. What does it feel like?\" She looked at him, and her eyes were hollow. \"I can't remember.\"\r\n\r\nKerel didn't answer. Just stared at her, expression caught between pity and fear.\r\n\r\nSalee stood. Her legs held, barely. \"Send in the next patient.\"\r\n\r\n\"Healer—\"\r\n\r\n\"Send them in.\"\r\n\r\nThe door opened. An old man hobbled through, leaning on a cane, face twisted with pain. Salee gestured him forward and pressed her hands to his back.\r\n\r\nThe pain flooded in. Sharp. Chronic. Old. She took it all, pulled it into herself, added it to the vast ocean of suffering she'd been carrying for two anneals. It didn't hurt. Nothing hurt anymore. She was just a vessel now. A conduit. Pain went in, disappeared, and she remained hollow.\r\n\r\nBehind her, Kerel was saying something. She couldn't hear him. The copper-gold glow was too bright, the warmth too consuming, and somewhere deep inside, a voice whispered: *If I take all the pain, no one has to suffer.*\r\n\r\nThe old man left. The next patient came. Then the next. Then the next.\r\n\r\nBy the time the clinic closed for the night, Salee's hands were covered in copper-gold veins, her skin darkened along the lines like someone had traced them with molten metal. When she tried to speak, her voice came out flat. Empty.\r\n\r\nKerel helped her to the recovery room and laid her down on the cot. She stared at the ceiling and felt nothing.\r\n\r\nNo pain. No exhaustion. No hunger. No fear.\r\n\r\nJust the certainty that tomorrow there would be more patients. More pain. More need.\r\n\r\nAnd she would take it all.\r\n\r\nEvery last piece.\r\n\r\nUntil there was nothing left of her but the glow.\r\n\r\n***\r\n\r\n### **ERIES: ENDINGS ARE NATURAL**\r\n\r\nVenn Eries stood at the edge of the mourner's circle and spoke the words he'd written three tides ago.\r\n\r\n\"All things end,\" he said. His voice was soft, barely louder than the wind. \"The proof-wall ends. The riser ends. The city ends. And we—\" He gestured at the bodies laid out on the stone, wrapped in cloth, waiting for the pyre. \"—we end too.\"\r\n\r\nThe mourners wept. Some turned away. But most stayed, listening, because Eries's words had a weight to them. A gravity that pulled you in even when you didn't want to hear.\r\n\r\n\"Endings are not failure,\" Eries continued. He stepped into the circle, hands folded, gray robes trailing in the dust. \"They are honesty. The world lies to us. Tells us we can hold on forever. That if we just work hard enough, pray hard enough, love hard enough, we can stop the collapse. But we can't.\" He looked at the bodies. \"And pretending otherwise is cruelty.\"\r\n\r\nA woman in the circle—young, red-eyed, clutching a child's toy—stood. \"Then what do we do?\"\r\n\r\nEries met her eyes. \"We let go.\"\r\n\r\nThe words hung in the air, soft and gray and final. Some of the mourners nodded. Others looked away, unable to bear it.\r\n\r\nEries felt the pull. The tug at the edges of his chest, like something inside him was unraveling. He'd felt it ever since the Piercing—a whisper, a call, a certainty that everything was falling apart and there was no stopping it. Only accepting.\r\n\r\nHe raised his hand, palm out, and let the gray shimmer come.\r\n\r\nIt wasn't bright. Wasn't warm. It was soft, faint, the color of winter fog. It spread across the mourners like a blanket, easing the tension in their shoulders, the grief in their chests. Not erasing it. Just... releasing it. Letting it be what it was without fighting.\r\n\r\nThe woman with the toy exhaled. Her shoulders sagged. She didn't stop crying, but the crying changed—became softer, gentler, less like screaming and more like rain.\r\n\r\n\"Release is a form of love,\" Eries said quietly. \"To force a grip past its honesty is violence.\"\r\n\r\nThe gray shimmer faded. Eries lowered his hand.\r\n\r\nAnd felt the cost.\r\n\r\nHis fingertips were cold. Not just cold—numb, like the feeling had been drained out of them and would never come back. He flexed his hand and felt nothing. The numbness was spreading. Up his wrists now, creeping toward his elbows. By next tide, maybe his shoulders. By next anneal, maybe his chest.\r\n\r\nHe was teaching people to let go. And in doing so, he was letting go of himself.\r\n\r\nA voice behind him—Maerys, the temple elder who'd expelled him three tides ago for heresy. \"You're killing yourself.\"\r\n\r\nEries turned. Maerys stood at the edge of the circle, arms crossed, expression unreadable.\r\n\r\n\"I'm helping them grieve,\" Eries said.\r\n\r\n\"You're teaching them to give up.\"\r\n\r\n\"I'm teaching them to accept.\" Eries stepped closer. His voice didn't rise. Didn't need to. \"The world is broken, Maerys. It's not coming back. We can fight it—burn ourselves out trying to hold things together—or we can let it fall and make peace with what comes after.\"\r\n\r\n\"And what comes after?\"\r\n\r\nEries looked at his hands. At the gray patina spreading across his knuckles, the places where feeling had already fled. \"Honesty.\"\r\n\r\nMaerys shook his head. \"You used to believe we could rebuild.\"\r\n\r\n\"I still do. But not by denying what's broken.\" Eries met his eyes. \"Rebuilding starts with letting go of what can't be saved. Otherwise you're just building on top of rot.\"\r\n\r\nMaerys didn't answer. Just turned and walked away.\r\n\r\nEries watched him go, then turned back to the mourners. They were lighting the pyre now, flames climbing into the gray sky. The bodies burned. The mourners wept. And Eries stood at the edge, hands folded, feeling nothing but the certainty that all things end.\r\n\r\nIncluding him.\r\n\r\nBy the time the pyre burned out, the numbness had reached his elbows. He tried to feel his heartbeat and couldn't. Tried to remember what warmth felt like and came up blank.\r\n\r\nHe walked back to his quarters—a small room on the western outskirts, cold and spare—and sat at his desk. Opened his treatise. Read the words he'd written yesterday:\r\n\r\n*\"To hold past honesty is to strangle. Release is the kindest gift.\"*\r\n\r\nHe picked up his pen and added a new line:\r\n\r\n*\"This will age you. And it already has.\"*\r\n\r\nThen he set the pen down and stared at his hands. Gray. Numb. Empty.\r\n\r\nTomorrow there would be more mourners. More grief. More endings.\r\n\r\nAnd he would teach them all to let go.\r\n\r\nOne piece at a time.\r\n\r\nUntil there was nothing left of him but the lesson.\r\n\r\n***\r\n\r\n## **CHAPTER 12: DRIFT ALIGN**\r\n\r\nThe next morning, Tiffani woke before dawn and couldn't fall back asleep.\r\n\r\nShe lay on the cot in Bram's guest room, staring at the ceiling, listening to the distant ring of hammer on anvil. Bram was already working. He always was. But the forge was quiet otherwise—no Chloen, no refugees seeking repairs, no city officials demanding explanations. Just the steady rhythm of metalwork and the faint hiss of banked coals.\r\n\r\nTiffani sat up, flexing her hands. The stiffness was worse this morning, joints grinding audibly with each movement. The blue-silver patina had spread past her knuckles now, tracing up toward her wrists like frost climbing glass. When she tried to make a fist, her fingers only curled halfway.\r\n\r\nShe wrapped her wrists in the compression strips she kept beside the cot, then pulled on her boots and stepped into the forge.\r\n\r\nJhace was in the recovery room.\r\n\r\nShe found him sitting on the edge of the cot, hands unwrapped, staring at his palms. The copper-gold mark from the Piercing glowed faint even in the dim light, and the vein-line on his forearm looked dull—lifeless, like the glow had burned out and might never come back.\r\n\r\nHe looked up when she entered. His eyes were shadowed, exhausted, but when he saw her something in his expression eased.\r\n\r\n\"You're awake,\" he said.\r\n\r\n\"So are you.\" Tiffani crossed the room and sat beside him. \"How are you feeling?\"\r\n\r\n\"Like I tried to hold the world together and failed.\"\r\n\r\n\"You didn't fail. You're alive.\"\r\n\r\nJhace made a sound that might have been a laugh. \"Barely.\"\r\n\r\nTiffani looked at his hands. At the dull vein-line, the tremor that wouldn't stop, the way his fingers shook even when he pressed them flat against his thighs. Yesterday she'd watched him collapse. Watched the light fade from his hands, watched his eyes go empty, watched him forget her name. And for five breaths she'd thought she'd lost him.\r\n\r\n\"Let me see your arms,\" she said quietly.\r\n\r\nJhace hesitated, then held them out. Tiffani stood, crossed to the basin in the corner, and filled it with water from the cistern. She brought it back, set it on the bench beside the cot, and pulled a clean cloth from the supply shelf.\r\n\r\n\"What are you doing?\" Jhace asked.\r\n\r\n\"Washing you.\" She dipped the cloth in the water and wrung it out. \"You've been covered in soot and ash for two tides. And I need to see the damage.\"\r\n\r\nJhace didn't argue. Just sat still while Tiffani gently wiped the soot from his forearms, revealing the copper-gold vein-line beneath. It ran from his palm to halfway up his forearm now, dull but present. She cleaned it carefully, watching for signs of infection, for places where the skin had split or the burn had deepened.\r\n\r\n\"It's not getting worse,\" she said finally.\r\n\r\n\"It's not getting better either.\"\r\n\r\n\"No.\" Tiffani set the cloth aside and looked at him. \"But you're still here. And that's what matters.\"\r\n\r\nJhace was quiet for five breaths. Then he said, \"I couldn't remember your name.\"\r\n\r\n\"I know.\"\r\n\r\n\"I couldn't remember why I was there. What I was doing. I just—\" He stopped, jaw tight. \"It was like someone had scooped out everything that made me me and left only the hollow.\"\r\n\r\nTiffani felt her chest tighten. She'd seen that hollow. Seen the way his eyes had gone empty, the way his body had gone slack, the way he'd collapsed like a puppet with its strings cut. And she'd thought—for five terrifying breaths—that she'd lost him.\r\n\r\n\"You came back,\" she said quietly.\r\n\r\n\"Because you were there.\" Jhace looked at her, and his eyes were raw. Honest. \"I couldn't remember my name. But I could remember you.\"\r\n\r\nTiffani's breath caught.\r\n\r\nJhace reached up, slow and deliberate, and pushed back her sleeve. The burn-scar on her right forearm was visible now—a perfectly square mark, faded with time but unmistakable. The scar from the scaffold collapse. The scar from failing Marisse.\r\n\r\n\"What's this?\" he asked, though his tone said he already knew.\r\n\r\nTiffani looked at the scar. At the mark that had haunted her for three castings, the reminder of every miscalculation she'd ever made, every person she'd failed to save.\r\n\r\n\"I miscalculated a load,\" she said. Her voice came out flat. Empty. \"Three castings ago. I was working under Marisse—my mentor—on a scaffold repair in the Glass Quarter. I measured the beam wrong. Told her it would hold. It didn't.\"\r\n\r\nShe felt Jhace's fingers trace the edge of the scar, gentle and deliberate.\r\n\r\n\"She fell,\" Tiffani continued. \"Six rods. Broke her back. She lived, but she couldn't work anymore. Couldn't walk without pain. And it was my fault.\"\r\n\r\n\"It was an accident,\" Jhace said.\r\n\r\n\"It was a mistake.\" Tiffani pulled her arm back, wrapping her sleeve over the scar. \"And I swore I'd never make another one. That I'd measure everything three times, calculate every load, never trust anything I couldn't prove. Because if I was perfect, no one else would have to fall.\"\r\n\r\nJhace was quiet for ten breaths. Then he said, \"You can't be perfect.\"\r\n\r\n\"I know.\" Tiffani looked at him. \"But I tried anyway. And it made me—\" She gestured vaguely at herself. \"—this. Closed. Controlled. Alone.\"\r\n\r\n\"You're not alone anymore.\"\r\n\r\n\"No.\" Tiffani felt something break open in her chest. Something she'd been holding closed for three castings, locked tight behind walls of calculation and precision and fear. \"But I'm scared.\"\r\n\r\n\"Of what?\"\r\n\r\n\"Of losing you.\" The words came out raw. \"I watched you collapse yesterday. Watched you forget who you were. And for five breaths I thought—\" Her voice cracked. \"I thought you were gone. And I couldn't—I can't—\"\r\n\r\nJhace reached out and took her hand. His fingers were still trembling, still cold, but his grip was steady. Real.\r\n\r\n\"I'm here,\" he said.\r\n\r\n\"I know. But what if next time you're not?\" Tiffani squeezed his hand, hard enough that her knuckles ached. \"What if next time I'm not fast enough? What if I lose you the same way I lost Marisse?\"\r\n\r\n\"Then you lose me.\" Jhace's voice was quiet. Honest. \"But you don't stop living because you're scared of losing people. You just—\" He stopped, searching for words. \"—you hold on tighter while you can.\"\r\n\r\nTiffani stared at him. At his raw, exhausted face. At his trembling hands and dull vein-line and the copper-gold mark that would never fade. At the man who'd burned himself out trying to save everyone and had almost died because he couldn't let go.\r\n\r\nAnd she thought: *He's right.*\r\n\r\nShe leaned forward, slow and deliberate, and kissed him.\r\n\r\nIt wasn't careful. Wasn't calculated. It was just her, closing the gap, pressing her lips to his, tasting soot and ash and something warm underneath. Jhace froze for half a breath, then his hand came up to cup her jaw, his thumb brushing across her cheekbone.\r\n\r\nThe kiss was gentle. Tentative. Real.\r\n\r\nWhen they pulled apart, Tiffani's heart was hammering and her hands were shaking and she felt more terrified than she'd ever been in her life.\r\n\r\n\"I'm scared,\" she whispered.\r\n\r\n\"Me too,\" Jhace said.\r\n\r\nThey sat like that for ten breaths, foreheads touching, breathing each other's air. Then Tiffani pulled back and looked at him.\r\n\r\n\"Heat-Rib 3 still needs us,\" she said.\r\n\r\n\"I know.\"\r\n\r\n\"And the Guildhall's going to use the demonstration against us.\"\r\n\r\n\"I know.\"\r\n\r\n\"And we're probably going to burn ourselves out trying to stabilize it.\"\r\n\r\n\"Probably.\" Jhace smiled, tired and honest. \"But we're doing it anyway.\"\r\n\r\n\"Together,\" Tiffani said.\r\n\r\n\"Together.\"\r\n\r\n***\r\n\r\nThey spent the rest of the day practicing.\r\n\r\nNot with metal. Not with broken flanges or bent risers or shattered proof-walls. With each other.\r\n\r\nThey stood side by side in the forge's back room, hands clasped, and practiced moving in sync. Jhace shifted his weight left; Tiffani mirrored him. She turned right; he followed.
